==================== START: market-sizing-quality-checklist ====================
# Market Sizing Quality Checklist

## Purpose

Validate that market sizing has used appropriate methodologies, documented assumptions transparently, and sized TAM/SAM/SOM with sufficient rigor to support investment and go-to-market decisions.

## What Gets Validated

**Task Reference:** `tasks/market-sizing-analysis.md`

**Used In:** ANALYSE phase for market opportunity assessment and validation

**Audience:** Strategic analysts, investment decision-makers, business planners

---

## Blocking Criteria (All Required)

### 1. TAM (Total Addressable Market) Is Sized ‚úì

- [ ] **TAM definition is clear**
  - What products/services included?
  - What geographies included?
  - What customer segments included?
  - Why these boundaries?

- [ ] **TAM is sized using appropriate method**
  - Top-down approach used (start from total market; drill down)
  - Bottom-up approach used (sum customer segments)
  - Or combination of methods
  - Method choice is justified

- [ ] **TAM sizing is supported by data**
  - What data sources used?
  - How recent is data?
  - How relevant to market?
  - Are sources credible?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
TAM DEFINITION:

Market: Enterprise software for supply chain optimization
Geography: North America (US + Canada)
Customers: Manufacturing companies with >$50M annual revenue
Time Period: 2025

Why These Boundaries:
- Supply chain optimization (not adjacent areas like logistics)
- North America first (geography of focus; represents 40% global TAM)
- Mid-to-large manufacturers (have budget; face pain point)
- 2025 (current year; base for projections)

TAM SIZING - TOP-DOWN METHOD:

Total Enterprise Software Market (North America): $140B
Supply Chain Software Subset: 8% of enterprise software = $11.2B
Optimization-specific portion: 35% of supply chain = $3.9B

TAM: $3.9B

TAM SIZING - BOTTOM-UP METHOD:

Target Companies: Manufacturing with >$50M revenue (North America)
Total companies in this category: 4,200
Average spend on supply chain optimization: $925K/year
(Based on analysis of 50+ company spends)

TAM: 4,200 √ó $925K = $3.9B

CONVERGENCE: Both methods = $3.9B ‚úì

DATA SOURCES:
- IDC market research (report from 2024): $11.2B supply chain software
- LinkedIn data: 4,200 qualifying manufacturers
- Customer interviews (30): Average spend $925K
- Gartner report: 35% of supply chain is optimization-focused

CONFIDENCE LEVEL: MEDIUM-HIGH
- Methods converge (strong signal)
- Data is recent (within 1-2 years)
- Multiple sources provide validation
- Some estimate work required
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfect methodology
- ‚ùå Multiple independent methods
- ‚ùå Academic precision
- ‚ùå Real-time market data

---

### 2. SAM (Serviceable Available Market) Is Sized ‚úì

- [ ] **SAM definition is realistic**
  - What portion of TAM is realistic to target?
  - What's our geographic focus?
  - What's our customer segment focus?
  - What's driving the boundary?

- [ ] **SAM sizing is justified**
  - Why this percentage of TAM?
  - Is this realistic for our capabilities?
  - Have we considered competitive landscape?
  - Have we considered market dynamics?

- [ ] **SAM is bounded by constraints**
  - Geographic constraints stated
  - Segment constraints stated
  - Product constraints stated
  - Capability/resource constraints stated

**What This Looks Like:**

```
SAM DEFINITION:

TAM: $3.9B (North America supply chain optimization)

Geographic SAM: North America; focusing on US only
Why: US represents 85% of North America opportunity; simpler to
execute than US + Canada split

Segment SAM: Manufacturing with >$50M revenue in automotive,
food processing, and pharmaceuticals (3 verticals)
Why: These 3 verticals have highest supply chain optimization needs
(regulatory, complexity, cost sensitivity). Together represent 60%
of mid-to-large manufacturers. Starting with sweet-spot segments
before expanding to other manufacturing verticals.

Product SAM: Demand planning and inventory optimization
Why: This is where our product strength is. Full supply chain
optimization includes procurement, logistics, warehouse management
(too broad for focused entry).

SAM CALCULATION:

Total TAM: $3.9B

Geographic adjustment: 85% for US only = $3.3B
Segment adjustment: 60% for 3 verticals = $1.98B
Product adjustment: 35% for demand + inventory = $693M

SAM: $693M

CONSTRAINT ANALYSIS:

Geographic Feasibility:
- US market is large enough for meaningful business
- Language/regulatory single country simplifies launch
- Can expand to Canada later once US established
- 85% focus makes sense

Segment Feasibility:
- 3 verticals chosen because:
  * Automotive: High complexity, mature, sophisticated buyers
  * Food Processing: Regulatory pressure, perishable goods urgency
  * Pharma: Regulatory + cold chain complexity
- Together ~1,250 qualifying companies
- Achievable target for sales team

Product Feasibility:
- Demand planning: Core competency (proven in customers)
- Inventory optimization: Extension (related capability)
- Rest of supply chain: Future expansion (not core now)
- 35% of market is realistic for focused product

CONFIDENCE LEVEL: MEDIUM
- Geographic focus is conservative and realistic
- Segment focus makes strategic sense
- Product scope is defensible
- But these are partially discretionary choices
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfect segment definition
- ‚ùå Conservative or aggressive positioning
- ‚ùå Proven market access
- ‚ùå Competitor agreement

---

### 3. SOM (Serviceable Obtainable Market) Is Sized ‚úì

- [ ] **SOM definition reflects realistic expectations**
  - What's achievable in 3-5 years?
  - What market share is realistic?
  - What's the basis for share assumption?
  - Have you considered competition?

- [ ] **SOM assumptions are grounded**
  - Market share assumption: What % of SAM?
  - Is this based on data or comparable companies?
  - Have competitive dynamics been considered?
  - Is growth rate realistic?

- [ ] **SOM takes market conditions into account**
  - Market growth rate included?
  - Competitive saturation considered?
  - Customer adoption curves modeled?
  - Distribution/channel constraints noted?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
SOM DEFINITION & SIZING:

SAM (Total opportunity): $693M

Market Share Assumptions:
1. Market growth: 15% annually (supply chain optimization growing
   faster than overall enterprise software)
2. Competitive share: Dominated by 3-4 players (relatively
   concentrated; some room for new entrant)
3. Our realistic share by Year 5: 8-12% of SAM
   (Based on comparable SaaS companies in adjacent markets)
4. Expected customers by Year 5: 120-150 companies

SOM CALCULATION:

Year 1: $693M SAM √ó 1% market penetration = $6.9M opportunity
Year 2: $800M SAM √ó 2% market penetration = $16M opportunity
Year 3: $920M SAM √ó 4% market penetration = $36.8M opportunity
Year 4: $1.06B SAM √ó 7% market penetration = $74.2M opportunity
Year 5: $1.22B SAM √ó 10% market penetration = $122M opportunity

SOM (5-year achievable): $122M in serviceable obtainable market

MARKET SHARE RATIONALE:

Why 10% by Year 5?
- Comparable companies (3 peers): Average 8-15% share of addressable market
- Our advantages: Superior product-market fit in chosen segments
- Our disadvantages: Late entrant; incumbents entrenched
- Realistic: 10% is achievable but requires strong execution
- Aggressive but not unrealistic

Why gradual ramp (1% ‚Üí 2% ‚Üí 4% ‚Üí 7% ‚Üí 10%)?
- Year 1: Establish product-market fit; build reference customers
- Year 2-3: Build sales team; establish market presence
- Year 4-5: Scale with brand and network effects
- Follows typical SaaS adoption curve for enterprise software

COMPETITION CONSIDERED:
- 2 major incumbents (each with 25-30% share)
- 3-4 specialized competitors (each with 5-10% share)
- Together control ~80% of market
- Remaining 20% available for new entrants + others
- Our 10% target represents ~50% of available share (aggressive but possible)

MARKET CONSTRAINTS:
- Adoption cycle: 6-9 months for typical customer
- Sales cycle: 3-4 months (enterprise software)
- Implementation: 3-6 months post-sale
- This limits Year 1 customer additions; affects ramp
- But by Year 3 we hit inflection with reference customers

CONFIDENCE LEVEL: MEDIUM
- Market growth assumption reasonable (industry growth 12-18%)
- Market share assumption realistic (comparable to similar companies)
- Gradual ramp makes sense (matches sales build and adoption)
- Competition recognized but market large enough for us
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfect share predictions
- ‚ùå Aggressive or conservative positioning
- ‚ùå Proven market access
- ‚ùå Detailed customer acquisition cost analysis

---

### 4. Sizing Assumptions Are Transparent ‚úì

- [ ] **Key assumptions are listed**
  - Market growth rate assumption
  - Market share assumptions
  - Customer segment assumptions
  - Product definition assumptions
  - Any other material assumptions

- [ ] **Assumptions are justified**
  - Why this growth rate?
  - Why this market share?
  - What data supports assumption?
  - What would change assumption?

- [ ] **Sensitivity to assumptions is analyzed**
  - What if growth is half expected?
  - What if market share is 5% not 10%?
  - What if customer acquisition slower?
  - How sensitive is SOM to key variables?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
KEY ASSUMPTIONS & SENSITIVITY:

Assumption 1: Market grows 15% annually
Base case: 15%
Optimistic: 20% (adoption accelerates)
Pessimistic: 10% (market slower than expected)
Impact on SOM:
  If 20% growth: Year 5 SAM = $1.46B; 10% share = $146M
  If 10% growth: Year 5 SAM = $1.10B; 10% share = $110M
Sensitivity: MEDIUM (could swing SOM ¬±20%)

Assumption 2: We achieve 10% market share by Year 5
Base case: 10%
Optimistic: 15% (we gain share faster than expected)
Pessimistic: 5% (competitive pressure limits us)
Impact on SOM:
  If 15% share: $1.22B √ó 15% = $183M
  If 5% share: $1.22B √ó 5% = $61M
Sensitivity: HIGH (could swing SOM ¬±50%)

Assumption 3: Customer acquisition ramps gradually
Base case: 1% ‚Üí 2% ‚Üí 4% ‚Üí 7% ‚Üí 10% per year
Optimistic: 2% ‚Üí 4% ‚Üí 8% ‚Üí 12% ‚Üí 15% (faster adoption)
Pessimistic: 0.5% ‚Üí 1% ‚Üí 2% ‚Üí 4% ‚Üí 6% (slower adoption)
Impact on SOM:
  If faster: Year 5 share reaches 15%; = $183M
  If slower: Year 5 share reaches 6%; = $73M
Sensitivity: HIGH (depends on sales execution)

Assumption 4: Average customer value stable at $925K/year
Base case: $925K
Optimistic: $1.2M (land and expand; higher ACV)
Pessimistic: $750K (price pressure; lower ACV)
Impact on SOM:
  If $1.2M: $1.22B √∑ $1.2M = 1,017 customers
  If $750K: $1.22B √∑ $750K = 1,627 customers
Sensitivity: MEDIUM (could shift customer count 40%)

SENSITIVITY SUMMARY:

Most sensitive to: Market share assumption (¬±50% swing)
Moderately sensitive to: Growth rate (¬±20% swing)
Least sensitive to: Customer value (¬±40% customer count)

Recommendation: Market share is biggest driver of value. Focus on
validating ability to compete and gain share.
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Formal sensitivity analysis
- ‚ùå Statistical modeling
- ‚ùå Scenario planning
- ‚ùå Perfect assumption documentation

---

### 5. Competitive Landscape Is Understood ‚úì

- [ ] **Competitors are identified**
  - Who are direct competitors?
  - Who are indirect competitors?
  - Who are emerging threats?
  - What's the competitive structure?

- [ ] **Competitive positioning is clear**
  - What's our differentiation?
  - Where are we vulnerable?
  - What's our competitive advantage?
  - Is positioning defensible?

- [ ] **Market dynamics are considered**
  - Is market growing or declining?
  - Is market consolidating or fragmenting?
  - What's the typical player size?
  - What are barriers to entry?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
COMPETITIVE LANDSCAPE:

DIRECT COMPETITORS (Pure Play):
1. CompetitorA: Focused supply chain optimization
   - Established 2016; strong market position
   - $250-300M revenue (estimated 30% market share in our SAM)
   - Positioned as "premium/comprehensive"
   - Strength: Comprehensive; strong brand
   - Weakness: High price; complex implementation

2. CompetitorB: Demand planning specialist
   - Established 2015; growing fast
   - $120-150M revenue (estimated 12-15% share)
   - Positioned as "SMB-friendly"
   - Strength: Easy to use; faster implementation
   - Weakness: Less comprehensive than needed by large enterprises

3. CompetitorC: Emerging AI-powered approach
   - Established 2020; venture-backed
   - $15-20M revenue (estimated 2-3% share)
   - Positioned as "AI-first optimization"
   - Strength: Modern technology; differentiated approach
   - Weakness: Unproven; limited reference customers

INDIRECT COMPETITORS (Broader Platform):
- SAP, Oracle ERP platforms (have supply chain modules but not specialized)
- Custom analytics/models (homegrown solutions in-house)
- Consulting firms (help optimize supply chain through services)

MARKET STRUCTURE:
- Concentrated: Top 2 players control 45%+ of market
- Mid-tier: 3-5 specialists control 20-30% of market
- Long tail: Many small players and custom solutions
- Growth dynamic: Consolidation (winners emerging, fragmentation declining)
- Entry difficulty: MODERATE
  * Capital needed: High ($20M+/year to build credible product/sales)
  * Time to market: 18-24 months to get serious traction
  * Brand/references needed: First customers are hard; reference customers critical

OUR COMPETITIVE POSITION:

Differentiation Strategy:
- "Fast, simple, effective" vs. "comprehensive"
- Focus on 3 verticals (automotive, food, pharma) vs. horizontal
- Strong data science (our team's unique strength)
- Vertical-specific best practices (not generic)

Advantages:
+ Our data science team is world-class (unique edge)
+ Vertical focus allows deep understanding
+ Could be faster/simpler than incumbents
+ Less established = can move faster

Disadvantages:
- No brand; no reference customers (yet)
- Limited capital vs. established players
- Sales team needs to build from scratch
- Customers prefer "safe" choice (incumbent)

Defensibility:
- Data science team is key asset (hard to replicate)
- Vertical expertise takes time to build
- Switching costs increase over time (data, integrations)
- But technology can be copied; need continuous innovation
- MOAT SCORE: MEDIUM (defensible initially, must innovate to sustain)

MARKET DYNAMICS:

Growth: 15% annually (faster than general enterprise software)
Why: Supply chain optimization is high ROI; customer pain is acute
Trend: Consolidation (winners emerging; others acquired)
Implication: Window exists for new player; but consolidation accelerates

Barriers to Entry:
- Capital intensity: MEDIUM-HIGH (need $20M+)
- Technical complexity: MEDIUM (hard but doable; many successful new entrants)
- Sales complexity: MEDIUM-HIGH (enterprise sales is difficult)
- Brand/credibility: MEDIUM-HIGH (first customers are hard)

Overall Assessment: Market is attractive for new entrant but competitive.
Success requires: Differentiation + excellent execution + capital.
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfect competitive intelligence
- ‚ùå Detailed financials for all competitors
- ‚ùå Unique differentiation (good enough sufficient)
- ‚ùå Unbeatable competitive advantage

---

### 6. Market Sizing Is Internally Consistent ‚úì

- [ ] **TAM, SAM, SOM hierarchy makes sense**
  - Does SOM < SAM < TAM?
  - Are percentages realistic?
  - Do definitions align across levels?
  - Is progression logical?

- [ ] **Sizing aligns with business plan**
  - Do revenue projections match SOM?
  - Do customer acquisition plans match SOM?
  - Do go-to-market plans reflect SAM?
  - Is everything coherent?

- [ ] **Sizing is documented clearly**
  - Could someone understand methodology?
  - Are sources cited?
  - Are assumptions stated?
  - Are calculations shown?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
INTERNAL CONSISTENCY CHECK:

TAM > SAM > SOM? ‚úì
$3.9B > $693M > $122M

Percentage Breakdowns:
- SAM = 18% of TAM (17.8% exactly) ‚úì
- SOM = 8% of SAM (17.6% of SAM) ‚úì
- SOM = 3% of TAM (3.1% exactly) ‚úì

Consistency with Business Plan:

Revenue Projection:
Year 5 projection: $40M ARR (assume $925K customer ACV)
= 43 customers by Year 5

SOM calculation suggests:
$122M √∑ $925K ACV = 132 customers by Year 5

Our plan: 43 customers = 3.5% of available SOM
Assessment: CONSERVATIVE (only capturing 3.5% of available opportunity)
Why conservative? Early years are about establishing product-market fit,
not maximizing market capture. Makes sense.

Sales Plan:
Year 1: 3-5 customers
Year 2: 12-15 customers
Year 3: 25-30 customers
Year 4: 35-40 customers
Year 5: 43-45 customers (goal)

Growth rate: 80%+ annually for first 3 years; slowing to 20% by Year 4-5
Assessment: MATCHES typical SaaS growth curve
Implied market share: Reaches 3.5% of SOM by Year 5
Assessment: REASONABLE given market concentration and competition

Go-to-Market Plan:
- Focus on 3 verticals (automotive, food, pharma)
- Direct sales team
- Start with 1 sales rep; grow to 3-4 by Year 3; 5-6 by Year 5

Assessment:
- 40-50 customers with 5-6 sales reps = 7-10 customers/rep/year
- Industry standard: 4-8 customers/rep/year for enterprise software
- Our plan: REASONABLE (slight upside to industry average, but achievable)

DOCUMENTATION QUALITY:

Methodology section: Clear, shows both top-down and bottom-up
Sources documented: ‚úì (IDC, LinkedIn, customer interviews, Gartner)
Assumptions listed: ‚úì (Growth rate, market share, ACV, adoption curve)
Sensitivity analysis: ‚úì (Shows impact if assumptions wrong)
Competitive context: ‚úì (Identifies major players and dynamics)
Clarity: ‚úì (Someone could understand and replicate)

Overall Assessment: Sizing is coherent, well-documented, internally consistent.
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfect alignment
- ‚ùå Conservative or aggressive positioning
- ‚ùå Audit-ready documentation
- ‚ùå Zero gaps between plan and market

---

## Enhancing Criteria (Improving Quality)

These items strengthen the sizing but aren't strictly blocking:

- [ ] **Industry expert validation**
  - Have independent experts reviewed sizing?
  - Do experts think sizing is realistic?
  - Are there different expert opinions?
  - How confident are experts?

- [ ] **Customer validation interviews**
  - Have target customers validated sizing?
  - Do they agree with market opportunity?
  - Do they agree with addressable segments?
  - Is there customer demand signal?

- [ ] **Comparable company benchmarking**
  - Have similar companies been analyzed?
  - What were their growth rates?
  - What market share did they achieve?
  - How does our plan compare?

- [ ] **Scenario planning included**
  - Best case scenario provided?
  - Worst case scenario provided?
  - Most likely scenario provided?
  - How do scenarios differ?

- [ ] **Update plan documented**
  - How often will sizing be revisited?
  - What would trigger update?
  - What metrics will be tracked?
  - How will real data be incorporated?

---

## Quality Checks (Before Approving)

Before confirming sizing is complete, ask yourself:

1. **Would an investor find this credible?**
   - Are methods sound?
   - Are assumptions reasonable?
   - Is documentation clear?
   - Are sources credible?

2. **Would this support investment decision?**
   - Is opportunity size attractive?
   - Is market realistic and achievable?
   - Are assumptions validated enough?
   - Would skeptic be convinced?

3. **Are assumptions stress-tested?**
   - What if market grows slower?
   - What if competition increases?
   - What if we capture less share?
   - Would business still work?

4. **Does sizing hang together logically?**
   - Do TAM/SAM/SOM make sense?
   - Does business plan align?
   - Are definitions consistent?
   - Is story coherent?

---

## Readiness Assessment

### ‚úÖ Market Sizing is Ready When

All blocking criteria are met:
- TAM is sized with appropriate method
- SAM is sized realistically
- SOM is sized with justified assumptions
- Assumptions are transparent and sensitive
- Competitive landscape is understood
- Sizing is internally consistent

**Market sizing can support go/no-go decisions.**

---

### üîÑ Market Sizing Needs More Work When

- **TAM unclear?** ‚Üí Clarify definition and methodology
- **SAM unrealistic?** ‚Üí Justify boundaries or adjust
- **SOM not grounded?** ‚Üí Base on comparable companies or data
- **Assumptions hidden?** ‚Üí Document and justify
- **Competition ignored?** ‚Üí Research and include
- **Internally inconsistent?** ‚Üí Reconcile differences

---

## Common Issues & Resolution

| Issue | Signal | Resolution |
|-------|--------|-----------|
| **TAM too large** | "$100B opportunity" | Drill down to realistic SAM; reduce scope |
| **TAM not defensible** | "How did you get that number?" | Show methodology; cite sources; explain calculation |
| **No SAM definition** | "What's realistic for us?" | Define segment focus; customer constraints; geography |
| **SOM too aggressive** | "We'll own 30% of market" | Compare to competitors; use comparable companies; be conservative |
| **Hidden assumptions** | "Where did this come from?" | List assumptions; justify; show sources |
| **No competition** | "We're uncontested" | Research competitors; understand market dynamics |
| **Numbers don't align** | "Your revenue plan doesn't match SOM" | Reconcile; adjust either sizing or plan |

---

## Post-Validation Actions

Once this checklist passes:

1. **Finalize Market Sizing Document**
   - Executive summary (¬Ω page)
   - TAM/SAM/SOM with calculations
   - Methodology and sources
   - Assumptions and sensitivity
   - Competitive context
   - Supporting appendices

2. **Present to Leadership**
   - Explain sizing to executives
   - Address questions on methodology
   - Discuss investment implications
   - Align on market opportunity
   - Get buy-in on assumptions

3. **Validate with Customers**
   - Share sizing with target customers
   - Gather feedback on addressable market
   - Validate segment choices
   - Confirm willingness to buy at scale
   - Build customer confidence in market

4. **Use for Planning**
   - Incorporate into business plan
   - Use to set revenue targets
   - Size sales team accordingly
   - Inform product roadmap
   - Guide go-to-market strategy

5. **Plan Ongoing Updates**
   - Identify metrics to track (actual vs. plan)
   - Schedule quarterly reviews
   - Plan annual updates
   - Incorporate real market data
   - Refine assumptions based on learning

---

## Sign-Off

**Market Sizing Completed By:** __________________ (Phase/Persona)

**Market Sizing Reviewed By:** __________________ (Analyst/Leader)

**Date Completed:** __________________

**Confirmed Ready for Use:** ‚òê Yes ‚òê No

**If No, specific issues:**

## Related Resources

- **Task:** `tasks/market-sizing-analysis.md`
- **Phase:** ANALYSE phase for opportunity assessment
- **Follow-up:** Financial modeling; strategic decision matrix

==================== END: market-sizing-quality-checklist ====================

==================== START: mid-point-synthesis-validation-checklist ====================
# Mid-Point Synthesis Validation Checklist

## Purpose

Validate that mid-project synthesis consolidates findings across initial phases, identifies cross-phase patterns, and confirms strategic direction is sound before moving to later phases.

## What Gets Validated

**Template Reference:** `templates/mid-point-synthesis-template.md`

**Used In:** Between ANALYSE and INSIGHT phases (after Phases 1-3 complete)

**Audience:** Strategic leaders, synthesis lead, decision-makers

---

## Blocking Criteria (All Required)

### 1. Findings From Each Phase Are Consolidated ‚úì

- [ ] **DEFINE phase learnings captured**
  - Problem definition summarized
  - Scope and boundaries clear
  - Success criteria articulated
  - Key stakeholders identified

- [ ] **SPLIT phase learnings captured**
  - Problem decomposition summarized
  - Root causes identified
  - Hypotheses tested and validated
  - Data gaps identified

- [ ] **ANALYSE phase learnings captured**
  - Market data consolidated
  - Competitive landscape documented
  - Financial modeling completed
  - Risk assessment finished

**What This Looks Like:**

```
PHASE LEARNINGS CONSOLIDATION:

DEFINE Phase Summary:
Problem: SMB market gap; competitors filling space
Scope: SMB segment of $2B supply chain market
Success Criteria: Identify viable SMB strategy
Key Stakeholders: CEO, Product, Sales, Finance

Root Causes Identified:
1. Strategic positioning (enterprise-only) created gap
2. Competitive entry (3 new competitors in SMB)
3. Market timing (window for entry is narrow)

SPLIT Phase Summary:
Problem Decomposition:
- Market opportunity sizing
- Competitive threat assessment
- Internal capability assessment
- Business model viability

Hypotheses Tested:
H1: SMB market wants vertical expertise (VALIDATED)
H2: Customers willing to pay premium ($60-80K) (PARTIALLY VALIDATED)
H3: We can build SMB product in 6 months (ASSUMED; test needed)
H4: Market will adopt from startup (ASSUMED; test needed)

Data Gaps Identified:
- Competitive win/loss data (don't have)
- Customer adoption curves (assumed vs. measured)
- Implementation complexity (estimated vs. validated)

ANALYSE Phase Summary:
Market Sizing:
- TAM: $3.9B (supply chain optimization, North America)
- SAM: $693M (automotive + pharma + food verticals)
- SOM: $122M (10% share target by Year 5)

Competitive Landscape:
- 2 major incumbents (30%+ share each)
- 3-4 specialists (5-10% share each)
- Market consolidating (winners emerging)
- Window for entry: 12-18 months

Financial Modeling:
- Investment needed: $3.5M
- Break-even: Year 3
- Profitability: Year 4+
- Unit economics: Strong (LTV:CAC 13:1)

Risk Assessment:
- Critical risks: Competitor entry; product timeline; pricing barriers
- Mitigations identified; contingencies planned
- Overall risk: MEDIUM (manageable)
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Exhaustive phase documentation
- ‚ùå Every finding detailed
- ‚ùå Perfect summarization
- ‚ùå Unanimous interpretation

---

### 2. Cross-Phase Patterns Are Identified ‚úì

- [ ] **Consistent insights across phases**
  - Do findings from DEFINE align with SPLIT?
  - Do findings from SPLIT align with ANALYSE?
  - Are there conflicting findings?
  - Is story coherent?

- [ ] **Themes and patterns recognized**
  - What are the key patterns?
  - What insights appear multiple times?
  - What's the core strategic insight?
  - What would a skeptic say?

- [ ] **Confidence assessment across phases**
  - High confidence findings (validated multiple ways)?
  - Medium confidence findings (validated one way)?
  - Low confidence findings (unvalidated assumptions)?
  - What needs additional validation?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
CROSS-PHASE PATTERN ANALYSIS:

Pattern 1: Market Gap Is Real and Durable
Evidence:
- DEFINE: Customers cite positioning mismatch (SMB wants enterprise features at SMB pricing)
- SPLIT: Root cause analysis identified strategic gap
- ANALYSE: Competitive entry confirmed gap exists; competitors filling space
Strength: STRONG (validated across all phases)
Confidence: HIGH (multiple data sources converge)

Pattern 2: Vertical Expertise Is Defensible Advantage
Evidence:
- DEFINE: Discovery indicated vertical expertise would be valued
- SPLIT: Hypothesis testing showed willingness to pay premium for vertical
- ANALYSE: Competitive benchmarking showed no other player owns vertical positioning
Strength: STRONG (validated across phases)
Confidence: MEDIUM-HIGH (competitive advantage less certain; could be copied)

Pattern 3: Timeline Is Tight; Window Narrow
Evidence:
- DEFINE: Stakeholder feedback indicated urgency
- SPLIT: Root cause analysis showed competitive threat is accelerating
- ANALYSE: Competitive landscape shows consolidation happening now
Strength: STRONG (validated across phases)
Confidence: HIGH (market dynamics are observable)

Potential Concern: Financial Model Assumes Faster Ramp Than Industry Average
Evidence:
- DEFINE: No discussion of customer adoption curves
- SPLIT: Hypothesis testing showed market interest but no real data
- ANALYSE: Financial model assumes aggressive customer acquisition (6/year ramping to 40/year)
Risk: Model could be overly optimistic
Action needed: Validate customer adoption curves with real data

Core Strategic Insight:
Market gap is real, defensible via vertical expertise, and has narrow window.
Successful entry requires: (1) Fast product development, (2) Vertical differentiation,
(3) Quick customer acquisition, (4) Strong execution. Feasible but risky.
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfect pattern identification
- ‚ùå All patterns documented
- ‚ùå Consensus on interpretation
- ‚ùå Fully resolved conflicts

---

### 3. Strategic Direction Is Validated ‚úì

- [ ] **Original strategic question is answered**
  - What was the question we started with?
  - Have we answered it?
  - Is answer clear?
  - Is answer supported by evidence?

- [ ] **Direction forward is clear**
  - Should we proceed? YES/NO/MAYBE?
  - What's the recommended path?
  - What would we be saying no to?
  - What trade-offs are we accepting?

- [ ] **Assumptions are articulated**
  - What key assumptions underlie recommendation?
  - Are assumptions stated explicitly?
  - What if assumptions prove wrong?
  - Are assumptions testable?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
STRATEGIC DIRECTION VALIDATION:

Original Question: Should we enter the SMB market?

Evidence-Based Answer: YES, we should enter SMB market via vertical-focused strategy.

Rationale:
1. Market gap is real and durable (multiple validation sources)
2. Gap is addressable via vertical expertise (defensible differentiation)
3. Window for entry is 12-18 months (competitive urgency is real)
4. Financial model shows viable path to profitability (Year 4)
5. Unit economics are strong (LTV:CAC 13:1; payback 7.5 months)
6. Risks are manageable (identified and mitigated)

Recommended Path:
1. Build separate SMB product (not embedded in enterprise)
2. Focus on 3 verticals (automotive, pharma, food processing)
3. Differentiate via vertical expertise (not horizontal breadth)
4. Target premium pricing ($60-80K ACV)
5. Launch MVP Month 6; market launch Month 12
6. Reach 10+ customers and break-even profitability by end of Year 2

What We're Saying No To:
- No to horizontal SMB play (too competitive; low margins)
- No to price-based competition (doesn't fit our strengths)
- No to internal product extension (requires separate focus)
- No to waiting (window will close; competitors moving)

Trade-Offs Accepted:
- Organizational complexity (separate product; separate GTM)
- Resource split (engineering and sales effort divided)
- Near-term profitability (investment in SMB)
- Narrower initial market (verticals vs. horizontal)

Key Assumptions Underlying Recommendation:
1. We can build SMB product in 6 months (ASSUMED; technical feasibility unclear)
2. Vertical expertise will command premium pricing (VALIDATED but limited data)
3. Market will adopt from startup (ASSUMED; adoption curves untested)
4. Competitors won't match our vertical focus (ASSUMED; possible but not certain)
5. Customer acquisition will follow our financial model (ASSUMED; depends on execution)

Assumptions Need Validation:
- Assumption 1: Test through pilot (build MVP; get feedback Month 3-4)
- Assumption 2: Validate through customer pricing testing (Month 2-3)
- Assumption 3: Validate through pilot customer acquisition (Month 7-9)
- Assumption 4: Monitor competitive intelligence (ongoing)
- Assumption 5: Track actual CAC vs. model (ongoing)

Go/No-Go Decision Points:
- Month 6: Product ready for pilot? (decision point)
- Month 9: Pilots showing positive adoption? (decision point)
- Month 12: Market launch shows traction? (decision point)
- If any "no": Reconsider strategy or pivot approach
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfect direction certainty
- ‚ùå Exhaustive assumption documentation
- ‚ùå Risk-free path forward
- ‚ùå Unanimous agreement

---

### 4. Confidence & Credibility Are Assessed ‚úì

- [ ] **Overall confidence level is stated**
  - How confident are we in direction?
  - Would skeptic accept recommendation?
  - What's the confidence level (high/medium/low)?
  - Why that level?

- [ ] **Credibility of analysis is assessed**
  - Is analysis rigorous?
  - Were alternatives considered?
  - Were assumptions validated?
  - Is analysis defensible?

- [ ] **Limitations are acknowledged**
  - What don't we know?
  - What assumptions are unvalidated?
  - What could prove us wrong?
  - What additional work would increase confidence?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
CONFIDENCE & CREDIBILITY ASSESSMENT:

Overall Confidence Level: MEDIUM-HIGH

Factors Supporting High Confidence:
‚úì Market gap validated across multiple phases
‚úì Competitive threat is observable (competitors actively entering)
‚úì Financial model is conservative (assumes below-market adoption)
‚úì Vertical differentiation is clear and defensible
‚úì Unit economics are strong (13:1 LTV:CAC)

Factors Limiting Full Confidence:
? Market adoption curves untested (assuming vs. measuring)
? Customer willingness to pay premium not fully validated (2-3 data points)
? Competitive response to our strategy unclear
? Product development timeline assumed (MVP build untested)
? GTM effectiveness uncertain (new sales model untested)

Credibility of Analysis:
Rigorous approach:
‚úì DEFINE phase: Comprehensive problem definition; stakeholder input
‚úì SPLIT phase: Hypothesis-driven analysis; testing validation
‚úì ANALYSE phase: Multi-source validation; competitive analysis; financial modeling
‚úì Cross-phase: Pattern analysis; assumption surfacing

Alternative Consideration:
? Price-based SMB strategy was considered and rejected (documented)
? Partnership approach was considered and rejected (documented)
? Horizontal SMB was considered and rejected (documented)
? Decision logic is defensible

Assumption Validation:
? Market gap: VALIDATED (competitive entry, customer feedback)
? Vertical opportunity: PARTIALLY VALIDATED (limited customer data)
? Financial model: ASSUMED (not yet tested in market)
? Execution feasibility: ASSUMED (product build not yet proven)
? Customer adoption: ASSUMED (real adoption curves untested)

Defensibility Against Skeptics:
"You're assuming adoption will be faster than industry average."
Response: We're modeling conservative adoption (1-2% Year 1); below average ramp
Support: Comparable companies show 1-2% initial adoption

"What if competitors match your vertical approach?"
Response: 18-month development lead; we'll own market during that window
Support: Vertical expertise takes time to build; first-mover advantage is real

"What if pricing doesn't hold at premium levels?"
Response: We have contingency; can adjust to $40-50K if needed
Support: Unit economics work at lower price; just slower path to profitability

Assessment: Analysis is defensible; skeptics would accept direction.
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfect confidence certainty
- ‚ùå All assumptions validated
- ‚ùå Addressed every possible skeptical question
- ‚ùå Risk-free recommendation

---

### 5. Next Phase Readiness Is Assessed ‚úì

- [ ] **INSIGHT phase is ready to begin**
  - Are we ready to move to insights/synthesis?
  - Do we have sufficient clarity?
  - Are there critical blockers?
  - Should we pause for additional work?

- [ ] **Work for INSIGHT phase is scoped**
  - What does INSIGHT need to do?
  - What are key deliverables?
  - What are success criteria?
  - What are time/resource needs?

- [ ] **Risks/dependencies for INSIGHT are clear**
  - What could impact INSIGHT work?
  - Are there dependencies from ANALYSE?
  - What assumptions does INSIGHT rely on?
  - Are they documented?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
NEXT PHASE READINESS:

Ready for INSIGHT Phase? YES

Readiness Assessment:
‚úì Strategic direction is clear (enter SMB via vertical positioning)
‚úì Key decisions are made (which strategy to pursue)
‚úì Analysis is sufficiently rigorous (defensible recommendations)
‚úì Confidence is adequate (MEDIUM-HIGH to proceed)
‚úì Assumptions are identified (can test in INSIGHT)

What INSIGHT Phase Needs to Do:
1. Develop competitive positioning strategy (how we differentiate)
2. Design business model for SMB (what does it look like?)
3. Create go-to-market strategy (how do we reach customers?)
4. Identify innovation opportunities (what else could we do?)
5. Develop narrative/messaging (how do we tell the story?)

INSIGHT Phase Deliverables:
- Competitive positioning strategy document
- Business model innovation options and recommendations
- Go-to-market strategy framework
- Innovation opportunity assessment
- Strategic narrative and messaging framework
- Preliminary roadmap for next 12 months

Success Criteria for INSIGHT:
- Clear positioning (distinct vs. competitors)
- Viable business model (profitable path forward)
- Credible GTM approach (customer acquisition strategy)
- Compelling narrative (story that resonates)
- Executive readiness (leadership aligned on direction)

Timeline for INSIGHT:
- Duration: 4-6 weeks (shorter than previous phases)
- Key milestones: Positioning (week 1); Business model (week 2); GTM (week 3); Narrative (week 4)
- Final synthesis and recommendation: Week 5-6

Resources for INSIGHT:
- Strategy lead: 1 FTE
- Product/GTM input: 0.5 FTE each
- External input: Customer calls (2-3); competitive expert review

---

Risks/Dependencies for INSIGHT:

Dependency 1: Market Adoption Curves
- Issue: INSIGHT will assume adoption model based on ANALYSE estimates
- Risk: If estimates are wrong, GTM strategy could be misaligned
- Mitigation: INSIGHT should validate adoption assumptions with customers

Dependency 2: Competitive Response Assumption
- Issue: INSIGHT assumes competitors won't match our vertical approach
- Risk: If competitors move faster, positioning becomes vulnerable
- Mitigation: Continuous competitive monitoring; prepare contingency positioning

Dependency 3: Pricing Assumption
- Issue: Financial model assumes $60-80K ACV can be sustained
- Risk: If market won't support premium pricing, margins suffer
- Mitigation: INSIGHT should test pricing with customer interviews

Dependency 4: Product Feasibility
- Issue: Assumes 6-month development timeline for MVP
- Risk: If product build slips, GTM and launch timeline slip
- Mitigation: INSIGHT should assume worst-case timeline; build contingency

All dependencies are documented for INSIGHT team awareness.
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Detailed INSIGHT phase plans
- ‚ùå All contingencies documented
- ‚ùå Perfect dependency mapping
- ‚ùå Zero blockers

---

### 6. Synthesis Document Is Clear ‚úì

- [ ] **Document is well-organized**
  - Sections flow logically?
  - Easy to follow?
  - Could be presented to board?
  - Professional quality?

- [ ] **Key insights are highlighted**
  - Most important findings stand out?
  - Recommendation is clear?
  - Rationale is easy to follow?
  - Can be understood in 20 minutes?

- [ ] **Supporting evidence is documented**
  - Key findings have evidence attached?
  - Sources are cited?
  - Assumptions are transparent?
  - Could be verified?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
SYNTHESIS DOCUMENT STRUCTURE:

EXECUTIVE SUMMARY (1 page):
- Problem: SMB market gap; competitors entering
- Analysis: Conducted comprehensive Define-Split-Analyse
- Recommendation: Enter SMB via vertical-focused strategy
- Confidence: MEDIUM-HIGH (supported by multi-phase evidence)
- Next steps: Proceed to INSIGHT phase; develop positioning and business model

KEY FINDINGS (2-3 pages):
1. Market Gap Is Real and Durable
   Finding: Strategic positioning created SMB gap; competitors filling
   Evidence: Customer research; competitive analysis; market dynamics
   Confidence: HIGH

2. Vertical Expertise Can Create Defensible Advantage
   Finding: Vertical positioning is unclaimed; can command premium
   Evidence: Customer interviews; competitive benchmarking
   Confidence: MEDIUM-HIGH

3. Financial Model Shows Viable Path to Profitability
   Finding: Unit economics are strong; profitability reachable by Year 4
   Evidence: Financial modeling; comparable company analysis
   Confidence: MEDIUM (dependent on execution)

STRATEGIC DIRECTION (1-2 pages):
- Recommendation: Build separate SMB product focused on 3 verticals
- Rationale: Addresses gap; defensible; financially viable
- Timeline: MVP Month 6; Launch Month 12; Profitability Year 4
- Investment: $3.5M capital required
- Confidence: MEDIUM-HIGH (multi-phase validation)

CROSS-PHASE SYNTHESIS (1 page):
- Patterns identified across phases (market gap, defensibility, timeline)
- Consistent findings across DEFINE/SPLIT/ANALYSE
- Assumptions identified for testing
- Risks identified and mitigated

NEXT PHASE READINESS (¬Ω page):
- Ready for INSIGHT phase: YES
- Key deliverables for INSIGHT: Positioning, business model, GTM
- Critical assumptions to test: Pricing, adoption curves, competitive response
- Timeline: 4-6 weeks for INSIGHT

Supporting Evidence:
- Appendix A: Market sizing and financial modeling
- Appendix B: Competitive analysis and benchmarking
- Appendix C: Risk assessment and mitigation
- Appendix D: Assumption list and validation status
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Polished presentation
- ‚ùå Exhaustive documentation
- ‚ùå Formal board package
- ‚ùå Perfect writing

---

## Enhancing Criteria (Improving Quality)

These items strengthen the synthesis but aren't strictly blocking:

- [ ] **Board-ready presentation**
  - Could this be presented to board?
  - Would board understand recommendation?
  - Would board feel confident proceeding?

- [ ] **Customer validation input**
  - Have key customers reviewed findings?
  - Do customers validate our assumptions?
  - Is customer perspective documented?

- [ ] **Competitive intelligence update**
  - Any new competitive moves since ANALYSE?
  - Does direction still make sense?
  - Are adjustments needed?

- [ ] **Team alignment assessment**
  - Does leadership team agree with direction?
  - Are there dissenting views documented?
  - Is consensus adequate to proceed?

---

## Quality Checks (Before Approving)

1. **Are findings credible?**
   - Would skeptic accept analysis?
   - Is evidence compelling?
   - Are assumptions stated?

2. **Is direction clear?**
   - Would someone understand recommendation?
   - Is rationale compelling?
   - Would they make same decision?

3. **Is next phase ready?**
   - Can INSIGHT proceed with confidence?
   - Are dependencies documented?
   - Are risks identified?

4. **Is synthesis complete?**
   - Have all three phases been summarized?
   - Are cross-phase patterns identified?
   - Is assessment comprehensive?

---

## Readiness Assessment

### ‚úÖ Synthesis is Ready When

All blocking criteria are met:
- Findings from each phase consolidated
- Cross-phase patterns identified
- Strategic direction validated
- Confidence and credibility assessed
- Next phase readiness confirmed
- Synthesis document is clear

**Can proceed to INSIGHT phase with confidence.**

# Persona Handoff Validation Checklist

## Purpose

Validate that persona handoffs transfer sufficient context, knowledge, and decision rationale so the next persona can proceed with full understanding and confidence.

## What Gets Validated

**Template Reference:** `templates/persona-handoff-template.md`

**Used In:** At every phase transition (after each of 7 phases)

**Audience:** Outgoing and incoming personas, orchestrator, continuity leads

---

## Blocking Criteria (All Required)

### 1. Work Completed in Phase Is Documented ‚úì

- [ ] **Phase deliverables are clearly stated**
  - What was the phase objective?
  - What were the deliverables?
  - Are all deliverables documented?
  - Is completion status clear?

- [ ] **Methods and approaches are documented**
  - How was the work conducted?
  - What methodology was used?
  - What tools/resources were employed?
  - Could it be replicated?

- [ ] **Quality of work is assessed**
  - Is work production quality good?
  - Did work meet phase success criteria?
  - Are there known gaps or limitations?
  - Would work pass review?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
PHASE DELIVERABLES:

DEFINE Phase (Strategic Analyst ‚Üí Strategic Specialist):

Deliverables Completed:
1. Strategic Context Brief
   - Status: COMPLETE
   - Quality: Ready for use (meets template requirements)
   - Key findings: Problem clear; scope defined; success criteria stated

2. Problem Definition Document
   - Status: COMPLETE
   - Quality: Comprehensive; multi-stakeholder input
   - Key findings: Root problem identified; not just symptoms

3. Stakeholder Map
   - Status: COMPLETE
   - Quality: Identifies influencers and blockers
   - Key findings: 12 key stakeholders identified; roles clear

Methodology Used:
- Stakeholder interviews (15 conducted)
- Business intelligence analysis
- Data gathering (historical performance, market context)
- Problem structuring workshop

Quality Assessment:
‚úì All deliverables meet DEFINE phase completion checklist
‚úì Stakeholder feedback is positive
‚úì Problem definition is crisp and specific
‚úì Scope is clearly bounded
‚úì Ready for SPLIT phase work
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Exhaustive documentation
- ‚ùå All supporting materials
- ‚ùå Perfect quality (good enough is sufficient)
- ‚ùå Unanimous satisfaction

---

### 2. Key Findings & Insights Are Clearly Stated ‚úì

- [ ] **Major findings are summarized**
  - What were the key discoveries?
  - What's surprising or important?
  - What would someone need to know?
  - Are findings well-documented?

- [ ] **Findings are supported by evidence**
  - Is evidence cited for each finding?
  - How confident are we in findings?
  - What's uncertain or needs validation?
  - Are confidence levels stated?

- [ ] **Implications are clear**
  - What do findings mean?
  - What should next phase do with findings?
  - What assumptions are they based on?
  - What should incoming persona know?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
KEY FINDINGS HANDOFF:

Finding 1: Market Gap Exists and Is Material
Summary: Strategic positioning created SMB market gap; competitors filling
Evidence: 
  - Customer interviews: 80% cited pricing/feature mismatch
  - Competitive analysis: 3 competitors entered SMB space last 18 months
  - Market data: $500M opportunity in SMB segment untapped
Confidence: HIGH (multiple validation sources)

Implications for SPLIT:
- Problem decomposition should focus on gap dynamics
- Root cause analysis should explore positioning vs. market fit
- Hypotheses should test whether gap is defensible or fixable

Finding 2: Vertical Expertise Could Be Differentiator
Summary: Competitors are horizontal; vertical positioning is unclaimed
Evidence:
  - Customer interviews: Automotive customers mention need for industry expertise
  - Competitive benchmarking: No competitor owns vertical positioning
  - Market research: Vertical SaaS companies command premium pricing
Confidence: MEDIUM-HIGH (limited customer data; indirect evidence)

Implications for SPLIT:
- Hypothesis testing should validate willingness to pay for vertical expertise
- Should test whether vertical focus is sustainable competitive advantage

Finding 3: Timeline for Action Is Tight
Summary: Competitive window is 12-18 months; consolidation is happening
Evidence:
  - Competitive intelligence: Market is consolidating
  - Customer feedback: Sense of urgency from prospects
  - Market trends: Fast-moving market dynamics
Confidence: MEDIUM-HIGH (observable trends; some inference)

Implications for SPLIT:
- Need to design solution that can launch quickly
- Rapid execution is critical success factor
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Every finding detailed
- ‚ùå Perfect evidence documentation
- ‚ùå Exhaustive implications
- ‚ùå Consensus on interpretation

---

### 3. Decisions Made in Phase Are Documented ‚úì

- [ ] **Major decisions are recorded**
  - What decisions were made?
  - Who made them?
  - What was the rationale?
  - When were they made?

- [ ] **Decision alternatives were considered**
  - What alternatives did we consider?
  - Why were they rejected?
  - What would have been different?
  - Are trade-offs documented?

- [ ] **Decision implications are clear**
  - How do decisions affect next phase?
  - What constraints do they create?
  - What opportunities do they enable?
  - Should incoming persona reconsider?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
DECISIONS MADE IN DEFINE PHASE:

Decision 1: Define Problem as Strategic Positioning Gap (Not Symptom)
Rationale: Customers weren't churning due to product quality or support; 
           they were churning because our positioning didn't address their needs
Alternatives Considered:
  - Frame as "pricing problem" ‚Üí Too narrow; doesn't capture full issue
  - Frame as "execution problem" ‚Üí Wrong root cause
Decision Maker: Strategic Analyst + Executive Sponsor
Timeline: Week 3 of DEFINE phase
Implications for SPLIT: Root cause analysis should explore positioning dynamics

Decision 2: Scope to 3 Verticals (Not All SMB)
Rationale: SMB market too broad; better to focus and dominate
Alternatives Considered:
  - Horizontal SMB approach ‚Üí Too competitive; low margins
  - Single vertical initially ‚Üí Too limiting; not enough market
Decision Maker: Strategic Analyst + Product Lead
Timeline: Week 4 of DEFINE phase
Implications for SPLIT: Hypothesis testing should validate if 3 verticals is optimal

Decision 3: Use Persona Handoff Approach for Knowledge Transfer
Rationale: Ensure knowledge doesn't get lost between phases
Alternatives Considered:
  - Single person leading all phases ‚Üí Creates bottleneck
  - No structured handoff ‚Üí Risk of context loss
Decision Maker: Orchestrator
Timeline: Week 1 of DEFINE phase
Implications for SPLIT: Specialist should use handoff to understand context
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Every decision documented
- ‚ùå Perfect alternative analysis
- ‚ùå Exhaustive implications
- ‚ùå Immutable decisions (can be revisited)

---

### 4. Alternative Approaches & Rejected Options Documented ‚úì

- [ ] **Alternatives that were considered**
  - What other approaches were explored?
  - Why were they rejected?
  - What would have been different?
  - Are trade-offs explicit?

- [ ] **Reasoning is clear**
  - Why did we choose this over that?
  - What were the deciding factors?
  - Would someone understand reasoning?
  - Are assumptions stated?

- [ ] **Path not taken is documented**
  - What did we not do?
  - Why not?
  - Could we revisit that path?
  - What would trigger reconsideration?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
ALTERNATIVES CONSIDERED & REJECTED:

Alternative 1: Address as Pricing Problem
Approach: Lower pricing to be competitive with SMB vendors
Rationale for rejection:
  - Interviews showed pricing wasn't the only issue
  - Feature gaps and positioning mismatch were equally important
  - Lowering price doesn't solve positioning problem
  - Would destroy enterprise premium positioning
What we learned: Problem is more complex than pricing; requires strategic response

Alternative 2: Serve SMB with Enterprise Product
Approach: Market existing enterprise product to SMB segment
Rationale for rejection:
  - Enterprise product too complex for SMB needs
  - Implementation time too long (120 days vs. needed 30 days)
  - Price point too high for SMB willing to pay
  - Would confuse brand (enterprise vs. SMB)
What we learned: Need separate product strategy; not just marketing adaptation

Alternative 3: Partner/Resell SMB Solution
Approach: Partner with SMB-focused vendor; white-label their solution
Rationale for rejection:
  - Loss of control over product roadmap
  - Customer relationships owned by partner
  - Revenue sharing reduces profitability
  - Strategic dependency on partner
What we learned: Need own product to maintain control and defensibility

Path Not Taken:
If we had pursued pricing-only approach, we would have:
- Missed root cause (positioning, not price)
- Damaged enterprise business
- Not addressed real customer need
Trigger for reconsideration: If vertical expertise strategy proves infeasible
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Every alternative considered
- ‚ùå Perfect analysis of paths not taken
- ‚ùå Certainty we chose correctly
- ‚ùå Immutable rejection (can revisit)

---

### 5. Assumptions & Uncertainties Are Surfaced ‚úì

- [ ] **Key assumptions underlying work are identified**
  - What assumptions did we make?
  - Why did we make them?
  - How confident are we?
  - What if they're wrong?

- [ ] **Uncertainties are documented**
  - What don't we know?
  - What needs validation?
  - What could surprise us?
  - What should next phase test?

- [ ] **Risk to assumptions is assessed**
  - If assumptions are wrong, how bad is it?
  - What assumptions are critical?
  - Which should be tested immediately?
  - Which can be validated later?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
ASSUMPTIONS & UNCERTAINTIES HANDOFF:

Assumption 1: Strategic Positioning Created the Gap
Confidence: HIGH (validated through customer interviews)
Evidence: 80% of customers cited positioning/feature mismatch
Risk if wrong: Problem might be unsolvable (if root cause different)
Testing needed: Minimal (well validated); could be revisited if market doesn't respond

Assumption 2: Market Will Value Vertical Expertise
Confidence: MEDIUM (limited data; mostly inferred)
Evidence: 3 customers said they'd pay premium; no transaction evidence
Risk if wrong: SMB strategy doesn't work; wrong go-to-market
Testing needed: Hypothesis testing in SPLIT phase (validate willingness to pay)

Assumption 3: Can Build and Launch SMB Product in 12 Months
Confidence: MEDIUM (never done SMB product before)
Evidence: Industry benchmarks; comparable company examples
Risk if wrong: Timeline slips; competitive window closes
Testing needed: Product feasibility study; detailed planning in SPLIT phase

Uncertainty 1: Actual Customer Adoption Curves Unknown
Issue: Don't have real SMB customer adoption data
Impact: Financial models are estimates; actual ramp could be different
Testing needed: Validate in pilot phase

Uncertainty 2: Competitive Response Timing Unknown
Issue: Don't know when/how competitors will respond
Impact: Window for market entry unclear
Testing needed: Continuous competitive intelligence; scenario planning

Uncertainty 3: Product Differentiation Durability Unknown
Issue: Vertical expertise could be copied; sustainability unclear
Impact: Advantage could be temporary
Testing needed: Competitive analysis; strategic defensibility assessment

Critical Assumptions for SPLIT Phase:
1. Vertical market will pay premium (should validate immediately)
2. Competitors can't match our approach easily (monitor continuously)
3. Market gap is fixable via new product (test through hypothesis)
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Every assumption listed
- ‚ùå Perfect confidence assessment
- ‚ùå All uncertainties addressed
- ‚ùå Elimination of uncertainty

---

### 6. Handoff Document Is Clear & Usable ‚úì

- [ ] **Document is well-organized**
  - Can next persona understand it?
  - Is structure logical?
  - Easy to find information?
  - Could be presented/discussed?

- [ ] **Key information is highlighted**
  - What does next persona need to know?
  - Most important items stand out?
  - Could understand in 30 minutes?
  - Gets to key points quickly?

- [ ] **Handoff communication is scheduled**
  - Is transfer meeting scheduled?
  - Will personas discuss live?
  - Are questions addressed?
  - Is clarity confirmed?

**What This Looks Like:**

```
HANDOFF DOCUMENT STRUCTURE:

1-Page Executive Handoff:
- Phase completed: DEFINE (problem clarification)
- Key finding: Strategic positioning created market gap
- Recommendation: Proceed to SPLIT with gap-focused problem decomposition
- Timeline: Ready for SPLIT phase handoff
- Next persona: Strategic Specialist

Detailed Handoff (3-5 pages):
1. Phase Summary
   - Objectives achieved
   - Deliverables completed
   - Quality assessment

2. Key Findings
   - Major discoveries (with evidence)
   - Confidence levels
   - Implications for next phase

3. Decisions Made
   - Strategic decisions documented
   - Alternatives considered
   - Rationale for choices

4. Assumptions & Uncertainties
   - Key assumptions stated
   - Confidence levels
   - What should be tested next

5. Questions for Next Persona
   - What SPLIT phase should explore
   - What hypotheses to test
   - What would change direction

Handoff Meeting:
- Duration: 1-2 hours
- Attendees: Outgoing persona, incoming persona, orchestrator
- Agenda: Review handoff; address questions; confirm understanding
- Outcome: Incoming persona confident to proceed

Post-Handoff:
- Handoff document archived
- Questions documented
- Follow-up items assigned
- Next persona ready to start
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Polished presentation
- ‚ùå Perfect documentation
- ‚ùå Extensive supporting materials
- ‚ùå Live video recording

---

## Quality Checks (Before Approving)

1. **Would incoming persona understand context?**
   - Could they proceed with confidence?
   - Would they know what to do?
   - Do they have sufficient information?

2. **Are key insights documented?**
   - Most important findings included?
   - Evidence provided?
   - Implications explained?

3. **Are uncertainties clear?**
   - What needs to be tested?
   - What assumptions need validation?
   - What could change direction?

4. **Is handoff well-communicated?**
   - Is document clear?
   - Will meeting transfer knowledge?
   - Would questions be answered?

---

## Readiness Assessment

### ‚úÖ Handoff is Ready When

All blocking criteria are met:
- Work completed is documented
- Key findings clearly stated
- Decisions made are recorded
- Alternatives/rejected options documented
- Assumptions and uncertainties surfaced
- Document is clear and usable

**Incoming persona can proceed with full understanding.**

---

## Sign-Off

**Handoff Prepared By:** __________________ (Outgoing Persona)

**Handoff Received By:** __________________ (Incoming Persona)

**Handoff Date:** __________________

**Confirmed Understanding:** ‚òê Yes ‚òê No

---

## Related Resources

- **Template:** `templates/persona-handoff-template.md`
- **Used at:** Every phase transition (6 handoffs total, 7 phases)
- **Purpose:** Ensure context/knowledge transfer between personas
