==================== START: strategic-brief-validation-checklist ====================
# Strategic Brief Validation Checklist

## Purpose

Validate that a completed Strategic Brief contains all essential sections with sufficient depth and clarity to serve as the executive summary of strategic work. This checklist ensures the brief can stand alone as a comprehensive strategic document.

## What Gets Validated

**Template Reference:** `templates/strategic-brief-tmpl.md`

**Used In:** DEFINE, STORY, and DECIDE phases as final executive summary

**Audience:** Executives, board, key stakeholders who need complete strategic picture without reading full analysis

## Blocking Criteria (All Required)

### 1. Executive Summary ‚úì

- [ ] **Opening paragraph captures full strategic situation**
  - What's the business challenge or opportunity?
  - Why does it matter (business impact)?
  - What's the strategic question being addressed?
  - Can someone understand the situation from this alone?

- [ ] **1-2 page summary is scannable**
  - Bold headers break up text
  - Key findings highlighted
  - Not dense paragraphs (bullets where appropriate)
  - Length is truly brief (1-2 pages maximum)

- [ ] **Tone is executive-appropriate**
  - Professional and credible
  - Evidence-based (not speculative)
  - Action-oriented (not descriptive)
  - Clear and direct (not academic)

**What This Looks Like:**
```
SITUATION:
We're experiencing 15% annual customer churn in our SMB segment,
representing $5M in lost ARR. Competitors are moving aggressively
downmarket with simplified, lower-cost offerings. Simultaneously,
our enterprise segment is highly stable (95% retention, $15M ARR)
with strong growth potential.

STRATEGIC QUESTION:
Should we expand into the SMB segment with a separate offering,
or maintain our focus on enterprise excellence?

KEY FINDINGS:
- SMB churn is driven primarily by pricing (60%) and feature gaps (30%)
- Enterprise customers are highly satisfied and loyal
- Market opportunity in SMB is substantial ($2B TAM; 25% annual growth)
- Competitors are establishing SMB presence; window may be time-limited
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Complete detailed analysis
- ‚ùå All supporting data
- ‚ùå Every finding detailed
- ‚ùå Polished presentation formatting

---

### 2. Problem/Opportunity Statement ‚úì

- [ ] **Problem is clearly and specifically stated**
  - Not vague or abstract
  - Business impact is quantified
  - Scope boundaries are defined
  - Root causes are indicated (without full analysis)

- [ ] **Problem statement could be understood by anyone**
  - Technical jargon is minimized
  - Context is provided where needed
  - Not assuming prior knowledge
  - Clarity over perfection

- [ ] **Problem connects to business strategy**
  - Why does this matter to the company?
  - How does it relate to strategic priorities?
  - What's at stake if not addressed?
  - Why now?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
PROBLEM STATEMENT:
We face a strategic inflection point. Our enterprise-focused
positioning has been highly successful ($15M ARR, 95% retention),
but it's left a significant market opportunity on the table. The SMB
segment we're not serving ($2B market, 25% growth) is being captured
by competitors with lower-cost, simpler offerings. Meanwhile, churn
in our existing SMB customers ($5M ARR) is running at 15% annually‚Äî
double our enterprise rate‚Äîprimarily due to pricing misalignment.

STRATEGIC SIGNIFICANCE:
This is not a tactical problem (which we could solve with pricing
tweaks). It's a strategic choice about whether we defend our
premium, enterprise-only positioning or leverage our success to
capture a larger market opportunity. The window for response is
limited‚Äîcompetitive positioning suggests 12-18 months before the
SMB market consolidates around fewer players.
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Root cause proof (just indication)
- ‚ùå Complete market analysis
- ‚ùå Multiple perspectives
- ‚ùå Detailed financial impact

---

### 3. Key Findings (Evidence-Based) ‚úì

- [ ] **Major findings are stated clearly**
  - What did analysis reveal?
  - Each finding is specific (not general)
  - Findings are supported by evidence
  - Findings connect to problem statement

- [ ] **Evidence for findings is provided**
  - Data/research backing finding cited
  - Confidence level indicated (proven, strong, moderate, exploratory)
  - Limitations acknowledged if relevant
  - Assumptions stated if used

- [ ] **Findings tell a coherent story**
  - They build on each other logically
  - Together they explain the situation
  - They point toward decision needed
  - They're not random facts

**What This Looks Like:**
```
KEY FINDINGS:

1. SMB CHURN IS DRIVEN BY PRICING MISALIGNMENT (High Confidence)
   Finding: SMB customers churn because we're 25-30% more expensive
   than primary competitors; exit interviews showed price cited as
   primary reason in 80% of churned accounts.
   Evidence: Internal churn data (CRM); 15 exit interviews;
   competitive pricing analysis
   Confidence: HIGH (internal data validated by multiple sources)

2. ENTERPRISE SEGMENT IS HIGHLY STABLE (High Confidence)
   Finding: Enterprise customers are highly satisfied (NPS 75+)
   and very loyal (95% retention); they value advanced features
   and support quality over cost.
   Evidence: Internal retention/NPS data; enterprise customer interviews
   Confidence: HIGH (clear trend in data)

3. SMB MARKET OPPORTUNITY IS SUBSTANTIAL (Moderate Confidence)
   Finding: SMB addressable market is ~$2B; market is growing at
   25% annually; competitors are consolidating around simplified
   feature sets.
   Evidence: Third-party market research (18 months old);
   customer feedback; competitive analysis
   Confidence: MODERATE (secondary research; may have aged)

4. COMPETITIVE WINDOW IS TIME-LIMITED (Moderate Confidence)
   Finding: Competitors are establishing SMB presence aggressively;
   market may consolidate within 12-18 months, making entry harder.
   Evidence: Competitive monitoring; customer feedback about
   competitor offers; market trends
   Confidence: MODERATE (based on pattern observation, not definitive)
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Exhaustive findings list
- ‚ùå Complete supporting data
- ‚ùå Perfect confidence levels
- ‚ùå All methodologies detailed

---

### 4. Strategic Implications & Insights ‚úì

- [ ] **"So what?" is clearly articulated**
  - What do findings mean for strategy?
  - What strategic choices are emerging?
  - What are we learning about market position?
  - What does this suggest about future?

- [ ] **Trade-offs are made visible**
  - What do we gain with each strategic option?
  - What do we give up?
  - What are we choosing between?
  - Why are trade-offs important?

- [ ] **Opportunities are identified**
  - What becomes possible with this understanding?
  - Where is growth opportunity?
  - What competitive advantage emerges?
  - What could we do that competitors can't?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
STRATEGIC IMPLICATIONS:

Our analysis reveals a strategic inflection point: we have successfully
dominated the enterprise market, but in doing so, we've become a premium
player with limited ability to capture the adjacent, larger SMB market.
This isn't a problem to be fixed with tactics; it's a strategic choice.

CHOICE POINT 1: Market Focus
- OPTION A: Defend our premium, enterprise-only positioning
  Gain: Simplicity; maximize enterprise profitability; clear focus
  Give Up: $2B market opportunity; future growth; market share

- OPTION B: Pursue dual-segment strategy (enterprise + SMB)
  Gain: Larger market opportunity; growth potential; market leadership
  Give Up: Organizational simplicity; resource split; near-term profitability

OPPORTUNITY:
If we can serve both enterprise and SMB profitably with distinct
offerings, we could double our addressable market and achieve
market leadership position. Enterprise excellence becomes our
strength; SMB value becomes our reach.

COMPETITIVE ADVANTAGE:
We have something competitors don't: proven enterprise success
(retention, NPS, product quality). We could leverage that to move
downmarket with confidence‚Äîtaking best practices from enterprise
and adapting them for SMB simplicity/affordability.
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Final recommendation
- ‚ùå Detailed implementation approach
- ‚ùå Complete strategic analysis
- ‚ùå Quantified opportunity sizing

---

### 5. Recommendations or Framing for Decision ‚úì

- [ ] **Clear recommendation OR clear decision framing**
  - If recommending: Option A is recommended because [rationale]
  - If framing decision: Here are the strategic choices and trade-offs
  - Rationale is clear and defensible
  - Recommendation connects to findings

- [ ] **Next steps are articulated**
  - What should happen next?
  - Who decides?
  - What's timeline for decision?
  - What work happens while deciding?

- [ ] **Success criteria are outlined**
  - What does success look like for recommended approach?
  - What metrics matter?
  - What's the target timeframe?
  - How will we measure success?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
RECOMMENDATION:
Pursue a dual-segment strategy with distinct enterprise and SMB
offerings. Rationale: This approach leverages our proven enterprise
strength, captures the substantial SMB opportunity before market
consolidates, and creates defensible market leadership position.

ALTERNATIVE CONSIDERED:
Enterprise-only focus offers simplicity but leaves significant market
opportunity to competitors. Risk: competitors establish SMB dominance;
we become legacy enterprise player.

NEXT STEPS:
1. Executive decision on strategic direction (this month)
2. If approved, detailed planning for dual-segment execution
   (next quarter)
3. Phase 1 execution: Foundation (Months 1-6)
4. Phase 2 execution: Launch and scale (Months 6-18)

SUCCESS METRICS:
- Enterprise: Maintain $15M+ ARR, >90% retention, NPS >75
- SMB: Reach $10M ARR by Year 2, >80% retention
- Combined: Path to $40M ARR by Year 3; profitability >40%
- Competitive: Establish market leadership in both segments
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Detailed implementation plan
- ‚ùå Complete business case
- ‚ùå Financial models
- ‚ùå Organizational design

---

### 6. Context & Audience Appropriateness ‚úì

- [ ] **Brief is self-contained**
  - Can be read standalone (doesn't require other documents)
  - Sufficient context for someone unfamiliar with history
  - Background provided where needed
  - Acronyms and jargon explained

- [ ] **Appropriate for stated audience**
  - If for executives: Focuses on strategy, decisions, implications
  - If for board: Includes competitive position, risk management
  - If for operations: Addresses implementation implications
  - Tone matches audience level

- [ ] **Length and format appropriate**
  - Executive briefs: 3-5 pages maximum
  - Detailed briefs: 8-10 pages maximum
  - Not padded with unnecessary detail
  - Easily readable (good spacing, clear sections)

**What This Looks Like:**
```
FOR EXECUTIVE TEAM:
3-page brief focusing on:
- Strategic situation
- Key decision needed
- Recommendation with rationale
- Next steps

FOR BOARD:
5-page brief adding:
- Competitive context
- Risk factors and mitigation
- Financial implications
- Governance/decision process

FOR OPERATIONAL TEAMS:
5-7 page brief adding:
- Implementation implications
- Timing and phases
- Resource needs
- Success metrics and tracking
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Multiple versions (one good brief sufficient)
- ‚ùå Perfect customization for each reader
- ‚ùå Exhaustive appendices
- ‚ùå Presentation-ready formatting

---

### 7. Documentation Quality ‚úì

- [ ] **Writing is clear and professional**
  - Grammar and spelling correct
  - Sentences are clear (not convoluted)
  - Paragraphs are well-organized
  - Active voice used where possible

- [ ] **Sources and data are referenced**
  - Where did findings come from?
  - How confident are we in data?
  - Are there caveats or limitations?
  - Can reader trace back to source?

- [ ] **Document is properly formatted**
  - Clear title and date
  - Sections are labeled
  - Key information is highlighted
  - Easy to navigate and skim

**What This Looks Like:**
```
TITLE: SMB Market Entry Strategic Decision Brief
DATE: November 13, 2025
PREPARED BY: Strategic Team
CONFIDENTIALITY: Internal Use Only

SECTIONS:
1. Executive Summary (1 page)
2. Business Situation (1 page)
3. Key Findings (2 pages)
4. Strategic Implications (1 page)
5. Recommendation & Next Steps (1 page)
6. Appendix: Data Sources & Confidence Levels (1 page)

DATA ATTRIBUTION:
All financial metrics sourced from [system name], validated by [role]
All customer feedback sourced from [number] interviews and
[data source], conducted by [team]
All competitive data sourced from [sources], dated [dates]
All market data sourced from [report name], published [date]
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Polished design/formatting
- ‚ùå Perfect writing (good enough is fine)
- ‚ùå Exhaustive citations
- ‚ùå Complete appendices

---

## Enhancing Criteria (Improving Quality)

These items strengthen the brief but aren't strictly blocking:

- [ ] **Compelling narrative arc**
  - Brief tells a story, not just list facts
  - Reader is drawn through logic
  - Emotional resonance with audience
  - Call-to-action is compelling

- [ ] **Stakeholder perspectives incorporated**
  - What do different stakeholders think about this?
  - How will this affect different groups?
  - Are legitimate concerns addressed?
  - Is there something for everyone?

- [ ] **Visual elements support text**
  - Key data presented in charts
  - Strategic matrix visualized
  - Competitive positioning clear visually
  - Visuals are simple and clear

- [ ] **Scenario planning included**
  - What if we pursue enterprise-only? Outcomes?
  - What if we pursue dual-segment? Outcomes?
  - What if competitors move faster? Impact?
  - What does success look like?

- [ ] **Financial implications sketched**
  - Investment required
  - Revenue potential
  - Profitability timeline
  - ROI indication

---

## Quality Checks (Before Approving)

Before confirming brief is complete, ask yourself:

1. **Could a busy executive understand this in 20 minutes?**
   - Is it clear what the situation is?
   - Can they see why this matters?
   - Can they understand the recommendation?
   - Would they be ready to decide?

2. **Does the brief stand alone?**
   - Someone unfamiliar with context could understand it?
   - No unexplained references or acronyms?
   - Sufficient background provided?
   - All key information included?

3. **Is the evidence compelling?**
   - Are findings backed by data?
   - Are confidence levels transparent?
   - Are assumptions stated?
   - Would someone trust the analysis?

4. **Is the recommendation clear?**
   - Do you state what you recommend?
   - Is the rationale clear?
   - Is it defensible against alternatives?
   - Are next steps obvious?

---

## Readiness Assessment

### ‚úÖ Brief is Ready for Use When

All blocking criteria are met:
- Executive summary captures full situation
- Problem/opportunity is clear and specific
- Key findings are evidence-based
- Strategic implications are articulated
- Recommendation or decision framing is clear
- Brief is self-contained and appropriate for audience
- Documentation quality is professional

**The brief can serve as authoritative executive summary.**

---

### üîÑ Brief Needs More Work When

One or more blocking criteria are unmet:

- **Executive summary still too detailed?**
  - ‚Üí Reduce to 1 page maximum
  - ‚Üí Focus only on essentials
  - ‚Üí Cut supporting detail

- **Problem not clear?**
  - ‚Üí Rewrite problem statement more specifically
  - ‚Üí Quantify the impact
  - ‚Üí Explain why it matters

- **Findings without evidence?**
  - ‚Üí Add data sources
  - ‚Üí State confidence levels
  - ‚Üí Cite research or data

- **Recommendation unclear?**
  - ‚Üí State explicitly what you recommend
  - ‚Üí Explain why (clear rationale)
  - ‚Üí Address alternatives

- **Not appropriate for audience?**
  - ‚Üí Adjust level of detail
  - ‚Üí Modify tone
  - ‚Üí Add/remove technical content

---

## What This Checklist DOES Validate

‚úÖ Brief is complete (all sections present)
‚úÖ Brief is clear and compelling
‚úÖ Brief can stand alone
‚úÖ Brief is evidence-based
‚úÖ Brief is ready for presentation to key stakeholders

---

## What This Checklist DOES NOT Validate

‚ùå Quality of underlying analysis (that's phase completion checklist)
‚ùå Correctness of recommendations (that's decision framework checklist)
‚ùå Feasibility of execution (that's execution plan checklist)
‚ùå Visual design or presentation polish

---

## Common Issues & Resolution

| Issue | Signal | Resolution |
|-------|--------|-----------|
| **Too long** | "This is 15 pages" | Cut to 5 pages max; move detail to appendix |
| **Too vague** | "I don't understand the problem" | Add specific numbers, examples, context |
| **No evidence** | "These are opinions" | Add data sources, confidence levels, caveats |
| **No recommendation** | "What should we do?" | State explicitly; provide rationale |
| **Too technical** | "Non-expert readers lost" | Add context, explain terminology, simplify |
| **Disorganized** | "Not following logic" | Restructure with clear sections; improve flow |

---

## Post-Validation Actions

Once this checklist passes:

1. **Finalize Brief**
   - Incorporate any final edits
   - Confirm formatting
   - Add any missing citations

2. **Prepare for Presentation**
   - Identify presentation audience
   - Create talking points
   - Prepare for Q&A

3. **Plan Distribution**
   - Who needs to receive brief?
   - What's distribution method?
   - Any confidentiality considerations?
   - Communication timing?

4. **Archive for Reference**
   - Save to project records
   - Document decision date
   - Reference for future phases
   - Learning capture

---

## Notes & Observations

Use this space to capture insights:

- **Most compelling finding in this brief?**

- **Key trade-off being made?**

- **Stakeholder concerns to watch for?**

- **For future engagements:**

**Confirmed Ready for Use:** ‚òê Yes ‚òê No

==================== START: strategic-context-brief-validation-checklist ====================
# Strategic Context Brief Validation Checklist

## Purpose

Validate that a completed Strategic Context Brief contains all essential context and discovery insights to serve as the handoff document from Discovery to DEFINE phase, enabling the Strategic Analyst to begin work with clear understanding of situation.

## What Gets Validated

**Template Reference:** `templates/strategic-context-brief.md`

**Used In:** Discovery phase as handoff to DEFINE phase

**Audience:** Strategic Analyst beginning DEFINE phase work

## Blocking Criteria (All Required)

### 1. Business Context is Clear ‚úì

- [ ] **Organization and industry understood**
  - What business is the organization in?
  - What's the industry/market?
  - What's organization size, structure, stage?
  - What's competitive landscape overview?

- [ ] **Current situation documented**
  - Current state of business (growth, challenges, opportunities)
  - Financial position (if relevant)
  - Market position (leader, challenger, niche)
  - Strategic priorities and constraints

- [ ] **Context sufficient for analysis**
  - Would analyst understand business without more conversation?
  - Are key industry dynamics noted?
  - Are organizational capabilities understood?
  - Are strategic constraints visible?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
ORGANIZATION:
SaaS company, Series B stage, $15M ARR, 80 employees
Enterprise-focused market positioning
Geographic focus: US and Europe
Product: Integration platform for enterprise data management

INDUSTRY:
Enterprise data integration market, $10B+ TAM
Growing at 20% annually
Consolidating around platform leaders
Key dynamics: Cloud adoption, API-first architecture, integration complexity

MARKET POSITION:
We're #3 player in enterprise segment (#1 = Competitor X, #2 = Competitor Y)
Differentiated by ease-of-use and integration breadth
Premium pricing strategy ($10K-50K+ annual contracts)
Strong enterprise customer success (NPS 75+, 95% retention)

STRATEGIC CONSTRAINTS:
Limited R&D resources (10 engineers for core product)
Distributed sales team (need to expand for growth)
Competing for enterprise attention (need product differentiation)
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Complete market analysis
- ‚ùå Detailed financial data
- ‚ùå Full organizational chart
- ‚ùå Comprehensive competitive intelligence

---

### 2. Challenge/Opportunity is Articulated ‚úì

- [ ] **What is being addressed is clear**
  - Is it a problem or opportunity?
  - What is the business impact?
  - Why does it matter now?
  - What's the context triggering the work?

- [ ] **Problem/opportunity is specific**
  - What specifically is happening?
  - Who is affected?
  - What's the scope?
  - What's at stake?

- [ ] **Initial perspective documented**
  - What does user think the problem is?
  - What are they hoping to understand?
  - What are their concerns?
  - What would success look like?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
CHALLENGE:
We're experiencing 15% annual churn in our SMB segment, representing
$5M in lost ARR. Meanwhile, competitors are moving aggressively
downmarket with simplified, lower-cost offerings. We need to understand
whether to defend our enterprise-only positioning or pursue the SMB
market opportunity.

BUSINESS IMPACT:
- Direct: $5M annual revenue at risk from SMB churn
- Strategic: Potential competitive vulnerability if SMB segment grows
- Opportunity: $2B SMB market opportunity (if we choose to pursue)

TIMING:
We're profitable and stable in enterprise. Decision needed in next
quarter before competitive window closes and resources needed to execute.

USER'S PERSPECTIVE:
CEO believes we should explore SMB opportunity but has concerns about
organizational distraction and execution risk. VP Sales thinks we need
to fix SMB pricing/positioning. Product team concerned about feature
complexity vs. SMB simplicity needs.

SUCCESS CRITERIA (INITIAL):
Clear understanding of SMB churn drivers
Quantified market opportunity assessment
Strategic recommendation on market approach
Execution roadmap if pursuing SMB strategy
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Root cause analysis
- ‚ùå Market research
- ‚ùå Detailed problem breakdown
- ‚ùå Stakeholder consensus

---

### 3. Stakeholder Landscape Mapped ‚úì

- [ ] **Key stakeholders identified**
  - Decision makers
  - Key influencers
  - Affected parties
  - Subject matter experts

- [ ] **Stakeholder perspectives captured**
  - What does each stakeholder care about?
  - What are their concerns?
  - What are their constraints?
  - Are there conflicts or differing views?

- [ ] **Engagement approach noted**
  - Who needs to be engaged in analysis?
  - Who needs to approve recommendations?
  - Who will implement decisions?
  - Communication and buy-in strategy?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
KEY STAKEHOLDERS:

DECISION MAKERS:
- CEO (strategic direction decision authority)
  Concerns: Execution risk, organizational distraction
  Position: Interested in SMB but wants data-driven decision

- Board (if strategic decision with capital implications)
  Concerns: Market opportunity, competitive risk, financial returns
  Position: Support growth opportunity if risk acceptable

OPERATIONAL LEADERSHIP:
- VP Product (product strategy, roadmap impact)
  Concerns: Feature complexity, product vision
  Position: Skeptical of SMB; prefers focus

- VP Sales (GTM strategy, sales capability)
  Concerns: Different sales motion, team capability
  Position: Believes SMB is urgent; we're losing customers

- CFO (financial impact, investment)
  Concerns: Profitability, capital allocation
  Position: Will fund if ROI justifies

SUBJECT MATTER EXPERTS:
- Head of Customer Success (churn insight, customer needs)
  Input: Exit interview themes, customer feedback
- Sales team (competitive intelligence, customer feedback)
  Input: Lost deal analysis, competitive positioning

ENGAGEMENT APPROACH:
- Initial discovery: CEO, VP Sales, VP Product, Head of CS (input)
- Analysis phase: Finance review, cross-functional interviews
- Recommendation: Executive team discussion and decision
- Implementation: Operational leaders lead execution
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Complete stakeholder mapping
- ‚ùå Detailed influence analysis
- ‚ùå Change management strategy
- ‚ùå Consensus building

---

### 4. Assumptions & Unknowns Documented ‚úì

- [ ] **Key assumptions about problem stated**
  - What are we assuming to be true?
  - Why do we believe it?
  - What evidence supports it?
  - What would prove us wrong?

- [ ] **Known unknowns identified**
  - What don't we know?
  - What would be important to learn?
  - What's the priority of unknowns?
  - How will we learn these?

- [ ] **Analysis questions framed**
  - What are the key questions to answer?
  - Are questions specific and answerable?
  - Would answers inform decisions?
  - Are questions prioritized?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
KEY ASSUMPTIONS:

Assumption 1: SMB churn is driven by pricing/feature misalignment
Evidence: Customer feedback in exit interviews; competitive positioning
Confidence: MODERATE (anecdotal; need data validation)
What would prove wrong: If other factors (support, brand) are primary drivers

Assumption 2: SMB market opportunity is substantial
Evidence: Market research reports; customer inquiry patterns
Confidence: MODERATE (market research can be dated; need validation)
What would prove wrong: If market is smaller or growing slower than expected

KNOWN UNKNOWNS:

Unknown 1: What specifically drives SMB churn? (Price? Features? Support?)
Why important: Determines strategic response options
How to learn: Exit interviews, cohort analysis, competitive analysis
Priority: CRITICAL (fundamental to decision)

Unknown 2: What is SMB market demand/TAM for our solution?
Why important: Determines opportunity size
How to learn: Market research, customer interviews, TAM analysis
Priority: CRITICAL

Unknown 3: Can we profitably serve both enterprise and SMB?
Why important: Determines feasibility of dual-segment strategy
How to learn: Unit economics analysis, business modeling
Priority: HIGH

Unknown 4: Would our current enterprise customers object to SMB focus?
Why important: Determines execution risk
How to learn: Customer advisory interviews, sentiment analysis
Priority: MEDIUM

ANALYSIS QUESTIONS:

Critical Questions:
1. Why are SMB customers churning? (root causes)
2. How large is the SMB market opportunity? (TAM/SAM/SOM)
3. What do competitors offer that we don't? (competitive gaps)
4. Can we profitably serve both segments? (unit economics)

Supporting Questions:
5. What would SMB customers need differently? (product requirements)
6. How would enterprise customers react? (retention risk)
7. What's the competitive response timeline? (market window)
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå All unknowns identified
- ‚ùå Complete assumption validation
- ‚ùå All analysis questions answered
- ‚ùå Perfect assumption clarity

---

### 5. Discovery Insights Documented ‚úì

- [ ] **Key discovery findings captured**
  - What did discovery conversation reveal?
  - What surprised us?
  - What themes emerged?
  - What points to investigate?

- [ ] **Customer/stakeholder voice included**
  - What did users/customers say?
  - What are they most concerned about?
  - What patterns emerged?
  - Are quotes/examples provided?

- [ ] **Red flags or opportunities noted**
  - What should we watch for?
  - What opportunities emerged?
  - What concerns were raised?
  - What areas need focus?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
KEY DISCOVERY FINDINGS:

Finding 1: SMB Customer Dissatisfaction Pattern
- Multiple SMB customers mentioned pricing as primary concern
- Described our solution as "enterprise-grade for SMB needs"
- Expressed frustration with feature complexity
- Noted competitors offer simpler, cheaper alternatives
Implication: SMB market willing to trade advanced features for simplicity/price

Finding 2: Enterprise Customer Strength
- Enterprise customers express high satisfaction
- Specifically value integration breadth and customization
- NPS scores are strong (75+)
- Long-term contracts and expansion deals common
Implication: Enterprise positioning is winning; don't lose focus

Finding 3: Market Dynamics Shift
- Multiple sources mentioned competitor downmarket moves
- Customer advisory board flagged competitive threat in SMB
- Competitive landscape consolidating around two tiers
Implication: Window for SMB entry may be time-limited

Finding 4: Product-Market Fit Questions
- SMB customers asking for simplified feature sets
- Enterprise customers want advanced features and customization
- Same product serving both seems challenging
Implication: May need separate product/positioning for SMB

CUSTOMER VOICE:

Quote from Churned SMB Customer:
"You're great for enterprise, but you're overkill for what we need. We'd
pay $500/month, not $5K/month. We found a competitor that gives us 80%
of the features at 20% of the cost."

Quote from Enterprise Customer:
"Your integration ecosystem is why we chose you. We need 15+ integrations
to work seamlessly. Most of your competitors can't do this."

Quote from Sales Team:
"We're losing SMB deals to Competitor Z on price every week. They're not
as capable, but they're 'good enough' and cheap. We can't compete on price
with our current positioning."

RED FLAGS:

Flag 1: Organizational capability concern
- Building/supporting dual-segment business is different from
  current enterprise focus
- Team has enterprise DNA; SMB is different
- Execution risk is real

Flag 2: Timing concern
- Market window for SMB entry estimated at 12-18 months
- Competitive moves happening now
- Decision timeline is critical

OPPORTUNITIES:

Opportunity 1: Leverage enterprise strength
- Our enterprise success is competitive advantage in SMB
- We could position as "enterprise-grade reliability for SMB simplicity"
- Reference customers could help SMB sales

Opportunity 2: Build integration ecosystem for SMB
- SMB increasingly wants integrations
- If we could provide 10-15 key integrations at SMB price point,
  would be compelling
- Different from enterprise integration depth
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Complete discovery notes
- ‚ùå All customer interviews transcribed
- ‚ùå Exhaustive stakeholder feedback
- ‚ùå Perfect organization of insights

---

### 6. Next Phase Readiness ‚úì

- [ ] **Analyst knows what to focus on**
  - Clear priority questions for analysis
  - Clear scope of analysis needed
  - Clear success criteria for phase
  - Clear what would be "enough"

- [ ] **Resources and access are arranged**
  - What data will analyst need?
  - Who is available for interviews?
  - What documentation exists?
  - Any constraints noted?

- [ ] **Analyst is prepared to begin**
  - Would analyst understand situation?
  - Do they know context and constraints?
  - Do they have stakeholder landscape?
  - Are expectations clear?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
FOCUS AREAS FOR ANALYSIS:

Priority 1: Root Cause Analysis (Why are SMB customers churning?)
- Conduct detailed exit interviews (15-20 customers)
- Analyze churn patterns by cohort, feature usage, pricing tier
- Compare churn reasons to competitor offerings
- Quantify impact of each driver (price, features, support)

Priority 2: Market Opportunity Assessment (How big is SMB market?)
- Validate TAM estimates through multiple sources
- Assess our addressable market (SAM) based on positioning
- Model our potential capture (SOM) based on realistic assumptions
- Compare to enterprise market opportunity

Priority 3: Competitive Analysis (What do competitors offer?)
- Create feature comparison matrix
- Assess competitive positioning and pricing
- Identify gaps we could exploit
- Assess competitive threats

Priority 4: Viability Assessment (Can we serve both profitably?)
- Build unit economics for SMB vs. enterprise
- Model dual-segment scenario financially
- Identify capability gaps
- Assess execution feasibility

AVAILABLE RESOURCES:

Data Resources:
- CRM system: Customer data, deal history, pricing, retention
- Customer interviews: Willing to participate (14 available)
- Exit interview notes: From past 6 months of SMB churn
- Sales intelligence: Competitive deal analysis, lost deal info

People Resources:
- CEO: Available for key interviews and context
- VP Sales: Available for competitive intelligence
- Head of CS: Has exit interview data and customer insights
- Finance team: Can provide financial analysis if needed

Access:
- CRM data: Full access available
- Customer interviews: CS team can coordinate; expect 1-2 weeks
- Board materials: If needed for context
- Competitive data: Sales has some intelligence gathered

CONSTRAINTS:
- Analysis window: 4-6 weeks (decision needed by end of quarter)
- SMB customer contact: Small number (50 customers); limited sample size
- Competitive data: Mostly public + sales intelligence (not comprehensive)
- Resource availability: CEO limited (quarterly planning other priority)

SUCCESS CRITERIA FOR DEFINE PHASE:

Minimum:
- Root causes of SMB churn identified and quantified
- Market opportunity sized (TAM/SAM/SOM)
- Competitive landscape understood
- Strategic choice options articulated

Ideal:
- Above plus
- Unit economics modeled for SMB vs. enterprise
- Execution feasibility assessed
- Stakeholder alignment beginning to form
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Complete resource specifications
- ‚ùå Detailed project plan
- ‚ùå All data sources mapped
- ‚ùå Perfect timeline for analysis

---

### 7. Documentation Quality ‚úì

- [ ] **Brief is clear and readable**
  - Easy to understand situation
  - Well-organized by topic
  - Key information highlighted
  - Not too long (5-7 pages maximum)

- [ ] **All required sections present**
  - Business context documented
  - Challenge/opportunity stated
  - Stakeholder landscape mapped
  - Assumptions and unknowns noted
  - Discovery insights captured
  - Next phase readiness addressed

- [ ] **Professional quality**
  - Grammar and spelling correct
  - Consistent formatting
  - Clear writing
  - Organized logically

**What This Looks Like:**
```
STRUCTURE:
1. Executive Summary (¬Ω page)
2. Organization & Industry Context (1 page)
3. Challenge/Opportunity Statement (1 page)
4. Stakeholder Landscape (1 page)
5. Key Assumptions & Unknowns (1 page)
6. Discovery Insights (1 page)
7. Next Phase Readiness (1 page)

QUALITY:
- All sections complete
- Key information highlighted
- No jargon without explanation
- Professional tone
- Ready for Analyst to use
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Polished formatting
- ‚ùå Graphics or visualizations
- ‚ùå Complete data appendices
- ‚ùå Executive presentation

---

## Enhancing Criteria (Improving Quality)

These items strengthen the brief but aren't strictly blocking:

- [ ] **Quantitative data included where available**
  - Customer numbers/segments
  - Financial impact numbers
  - Churn rates and trends
  - Market size estimates

- [ ] **Customer perspective is rich**
  - Specific customer quotes included
  - Customer pain points articulated
  - Customer needs visible
  - Customer urgency understood

- [ ] **Organizational readiness assessed**
  - Do we have capability for solution?
  - What capability gaps exist?
  - Are resource constraints noted?
  - Is organizational appetite clear?

- [ ] **Timeline and urgency clear**
  - Why does this matter now?
  - What's the decision timeline?
  - What's the market window?
  - What are consequences of delay?

---

## Quality Checks

Before confirming brief is complete, ask yourself:

1. **Would Analyst understand situation without more conversation?**
   - Business context clear?
   - Challenge understood?
   - Stakeholder landscape visible?
   - Next steps obvious?

2. **Does Analyst have what they need to begin?**
   - Key questions defined?
   - Analysis priorities clear?
   - Resources identified?
   - Success criteria articulated?

3. **Is brief credible?**
   - Based on discovery conversation?
   - Reflects stakeholder perspectives?
   - Includes customer voice?
   - Balanced (not biased)?

4. **Is context preserved?**
   - Could someone 6 months later understand?
   - Are assumptions documented?
   - Are sources noted?
   - Is discovery captured?

---

## Readiness Assessment

### ‚úÖ Brief is Ready When

All blocking criteria are met:
- Business context is clear
- Challenge/opportunity is articulated
- Stakeholder landscape is mapped
- Assumptions and unknowns are documented
- Discovery insights are captured
- Next phase readiness is addressed
- Documentation quality is professional

**Analyst can begin DEFINE phase with confidence.**

---

### üîÑ Brief Needs More Work When

- **Context unclear?** ‚Üí Provide more business/industry background
- **Challenge vague?** ‚Üí Be more specific about problem/opportunity
- **Stakeholders not mapped?** ‚Üí Identify decision-makers and influencers
- **Assumptions missing?** ‚Üí Document what you believe to be true
- **Insights superficial?** ‚Üí Capture deeper themes from discovery
- **Not ready for next phase?** ‚Üí Clarify priorities and success criteria

---

## Common Issues & Resolution

| Issue | Signal | Resolution |
|-------|--------|-----------|
| **Too detailed** | "20 pages of notes" | Summarize; focus on essentials |
| **Too vague** | "Unclear what we're analyzing" | Be more specific and concrete |
| **Missing context** | "Who are the stakeholders?" | Map stakeholder landscape |
| **No discovery captured** | "What did we learn?" | Document key findings and themes |
| **Not actionable** | "What should Analyst do?" | Define clear priority questions |
| **Biased perspective** | "Reads like advocacy" | Include multiple stakeholder views |

---

## Post-Validation Actions

Once this checklist passes:

1. **Share with DEFINE Analyst**
   - Brief is provided at phase start
   - Analyst reviews and confirms understanding
   - Clarify any questions
   - Analyst confirms ready to begin

2. **Establish Success Criteria**
   - Confirm what "complete DEFINE" looks like
   - Establish completion timeline
   - Note dependencies or constraints
   - Plan phase completion review

3. **Prepare Handoff**
   - Archive discovery context
   - Prepare for phase transition
   - Brief leadership on progress
   - Plan next phase logistics

4. **Keep for Reference**
   - Save as reference throughout project
   - Update if context changes
   - Reference in final synthesis
   - Use for learning/reflection

---

==================== START: strategic-decision-matrix-validation-checklist ====================
# Strategic Decision Matrix Validation Checklist

## Purpose

Validate that a completed Strategic Decision Matrix provides a rigorous framework for evaluating strategic options with clear criteria, transparent scoring, and defensible recommendations.

## What Gets Validated

**Template Reference:** `templates/strategic-decision-matrix.md`

**Used In:** DECIDE phase for systematic option evaluation

**Audience:** Executives, decision-makers evaluating strategic alternatives

## Blocking Criteria (All Required)

### 1. Decision Question is Clear ‚úì

- [ ] **Decision being made is explicit**
  - What exactly are we deciding?
  - Is decision clearly stated?
  - Is decision specific (not vague)?
  - Would different people understand same decision?

- [ ] **Context for decision provided**
  - Why is this decision needed now?
  - What prompted the decision?
  - What's at stake?
  - What's the timeline?

- [ ] **Decision authority clear**
  - Who will make final decision?
  - When will decision be made?
  - What input is needed?
  - What happens after decision?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
DECISION BEING MADE:
"Should we enter the SMB market segment, and if so, how?"

CONTEXT:
- Current state: Enterprise-focused, highly profitable
- Problem: SMB segment is large ($2B) and growing; competitors
  moving downmarket; our SMB customers churning at 15%
- Opportunity: Could serve both enterprise and SMB profitably
- Timing: Market window for SMB entry is 12-18 months before
  competitors consolidate market
- Risk: Dual focus could distract from enterprise excellence

DECISION AUTHORITY:
- Decision-maker: CEO (with Board input)
- Timeline: This month
- Input needed: Executive leadership perspectives; Board guidance
- Next step: If approved, detailed execution planning
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Complete context (summary sufficient)
- ‚ùå Detailed decision tree
- ‚ùå Complete stakeholder input

---

### 2. Strategic Options are Distinct & Complete ‚úì

- [ ] **All viable options identified**
  - What are real strategic choices?
  - Are options meaningfully different?
  - Is "do nothing" option included?
  - Are there any other viable paths?

- [ ] **Options are clearly described**
  - What specifically would each option mean?
  - What would be required to execute?
  - What would success look like for each?
  - Are implications of each clear?

- [ ] **Options are mutually exclusive or combinations identified**
  - Can we do multiple options? (If yes, note combinations)
  - If mutually exclusive, are trade-offs clear?
  - Is timing of decision right? (Can we revisit later?)

**What This Looks Like:**
```
OPTION 1: DO NOTHING / ENTERPRISE-ONLY FOCUS
What it means: Continue current enterprise-only strategy
Requirements: Maintain current product, sales, marketing focus
Success looks like: Enterprise grows to $25M ARR; maintain >90% retention
Implications: Miss $2B market opportunity; competitors establish SMB dominance

OPTION 2: DUAL-SEGMENT STRATEGY (Recommended)
What it means: Create separate SMB offering while maintaining enterprise excellence
Requirements: New product team, separate GTM, $5M investment, 18-month timeline
Success looks like: Enterprise $25M + SMB $10M by Year 2
Implications: More complex organization, split resources, greater growth potential

OPTION 3: ACQUIRE SMB COMPETITOR
What it means: Purchase existing SMB solution; integrate into portfolio
Requirements: $50M acquisition, integration team, 12-month integration
Success looks like: Combined entity $30M by Year 2; cost synergies realized
Implications: High cash outlay, integration complexity, faster entry

COMBINATIONS:
- Option 2 + 3: Acquire to accelerate SMB entry while building separate product
  (Could work but most expensive)
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Every possible option
- ‚ùå Detailed business plans for each
- ‚ùå Complete specifications
- ‚ùå Exhaustive implications

---

### 3. Decision Criteria are Relevant & Weighted ‚úì

- [ ] **Criteria reflect what matters**
  - What factors should inform this decision?
  - Are criteria aligned with company strategy?
  - Would executives agree these are right criteria?
  - Are all important factors covered?

- [ ] **Criteria are weighted appropriately**
  - Does weight reflect importance?
  - Are weights explicit (not just implicit)?
  - Would decision-makers agree with weights?
  - Do weights total to 100%?

- [ ] **Criteria are measurable or assessable**
  - Can we evaluate each option on each criterion?
  - Are criteria vague or specific?
  - Would different people score options similarly?
  - Can we defend scoring?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
STRATEGIC CRITERIA & WEIGHTS:

1. MARKET OPPORTUNITY (Weight: 30%) ‚Äî How much growth potential?
   Definition: TAM size, growth rate, competitive window

2. FINANCIAL RETURNS (Weight: 25%) ‚Äî Is ROI attractive?
   Definition: Investment required, revenue potential, profitability timeline

3. COMPETITIVE POSITIONING (Weight: 25%) ‚Äî Improves competitive position?
   Definition: Defensible advantage, market leadership, moat strength

4. ORGANIZATIONAL FEASIBILITY (Weight: 20%) ‚Äî Can we execute?
   Definition: Resource availability, capability gaps, execution risk

TOTAL WEIGHTS: 30% + 25% + 25% + 20% = 100% ‚úì

WEIGHTING RATIONALE:
- Market opportunity weighted highest because market entry window
  is time-limited; competitive dynamics changing fast
- Financial returns weighted highly because we're profitable
  company; can't justify massive investment
- Competitive positioning matters because we want defensible
  advantage, not temporary market share gain
- Organizational feasibility important but acknowledged: we can
  build capability; execution is more flexible than market timing
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfect criteria
- ‚ùå Unanimous agreement on weights
- ‚ùå Quantified measurement
- ‚ùå Complex weighting schemes

---

### 4. Options Evaluated Against Criteria ‚úì

- [ ] **Each option scored on each criterion**
  - Is scoring clear and transparent?
  - Can we see how options compare?
  - Is scoring methodology explained?
  - Would someone understand the scoring?

- [ ] **Scoring is consistent**
  - Are scores on same scale?
  - Is scoring logic consistent across options?
  - Could someone else score options the same way?
  - Any outlier scores explained?

- [ ] **Supporting rationale provided**
  - Why did we score each option that way?
  - What evidence supports each score?
  - What trade-offs exist?
  - Are any scores uncertain?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
DECISION MATRIX:

CRITERION                | Option 1    | Option 2    | Option 3
                        | (Do Nothing) | (Dual-Seg)  | (Acquire)
Market Opportunity      | 2/10        | 9/10        | 7/10
  (Weight: 30%)         | Limited to  | Captures    | Gains SMB
                        | enterprise  | $2B market  | access but
                        | growth      |             | limited upside

Financial Returns       | 8/10        | 8/10        | 6/10
  (Weight: 25%)         | High margin | Balanced    | High investment,
                        | but limited | growth/     | uncertain
                        | growth      | margin      | integration costs

Competitive Positioning | 5/10        | 9/10        | 7/10
  (Weight: 25%)         | Vulnerable  | Market      | Established SMB
                        | to downmkt  | leadership  | but integration
                        | moves       | position    | challenges

Org Feasibility         | 9/10        | 6/10        | 5/10
  (Weight: 20%)         | No change;  | Dual focus; | High complexity;
                        | simple      | some risk   | major integration

WEIGHTED SCORES:
Option 1: (2√ó0.30) + (8√ó0.25) + (5√ó0.25) + (9√ó0.20) = 0.6 + 2.0 + 1.25 + 1.8 = 5.65/10
Option 2: (9√ó0.30) + (8√ó0.25) + (9√ó0.25) + (6√ó0.20) = 2.7 + 2.0 + 2.25 + 1.2 = 8.15/10 ‚Üê HIGHEST
Option 3: (7√ó0.30) + (6√ó0.25) + (7√ó0.25) + (5√ó0.20) = 2.1 + 1.5 + 1.75 + 1.0 = 6.35/10
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Quantified objective scoring
- ‚ùå Perfect consistency
- ‚ùå Unanimous agreement on scores
- ‚ùå Complex statistical analysis

---

### 5. Trade-Offs Are Explicitly Addressed ‚úì

- [ ] **Trade-offs are stated clearly**
  - What do we gain with each option?
  - What do we give up?
  - What's the key trade-off?
  - Is there a clear winner or do trade-offs matter?

- [ ] **Strategic choices are visible**
  - Is there a fundamental choice being made?
  - Would different stakeholders weight criteria differently?
  - Are there legitimate alternative views?
  - What would change someone's mind about this?

- [ ] **Sensitivity analysis considered**
  - Would changing weights change outcome?
  - How sensitive is recommendation to key assumptions?
  - What would we need to be wrong about?
  - How confident are we in recommendation?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
KEY TRADE-OFFS:

Trade-off 1: Growth vs. Simplicity
- Option 2 wins on growth opportunity (9 vs. 2)
- Option 1 wins on organizational simplicity (9 vs. 6)
- Choice: Do we prioritize market opportunity or simplicity?
- Implication: If we choose Option 2, we accept complexity

Trade-off 2: Speed vs. Certainty
- Option 3 fastest to market (acquisition)
- Option 2 takes longer but more certainty (organic build)
- Choice: Do we value speed or control?
- Implication: If we choose Option 3, we accept integration risk

Trade-off 3: Capital vs. Upside
- Option 3 requires $50M; Option 2 requires $5M
- Option 3 faster returns; Option 2 longer payback
- Choice: How much capital can we deploy? How much risk?
- Implication: If we choose Option 3, large capital outlay

SENSITIVITY ANALYSIS:

"What if market opportunity is smaller than estimated?"
- If SMB market is $1B (vs. $2B), Option 2 score drops to 7.5/10
- Still wins vs. Option 1 (5.65) but closer to Option 3 (6.35)
- Conclusion: Even with downside, Option 2 wins

"What if we can't execute dual focus?"
- If organizational feasibility for Option 2 drops to 4/10
- Option 2 score drops to 7.25/10; still wins
- But risk is real; need strong governance

"What if competition moves faster?"
- If we lose first-mover advantage in SMB (Option 2 drops to 7/10)
- Option 2 still wins but margin tightens
- Timing is critical; can't wait
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Exhaustive sensitivity analysis
- ‚ùå Statistical confidence intervals
- ‚ùå Perfect quantification
- ‚ùå Complete uncertainty assessment

---

### 6. Recommendation is Clear & Justified ‚úì

- [ ] **Clear recommendation from matrix**
  - Which option is recommended?
  - Why this option? (Clear rationale from matrix)
  - What's the confidence level?
  - What could change recommendation?

- [ ] **Recommendation is defensible**
  - Would someone understand why?
  - Is logic sound?
  - Are alternatives acknowledged?
  - What would we need to be wrong about?

- [ ] **Next steps are clear**
  - If recommendation approved, what happens next?
  - Who decides?
  - When is decision finalized?
  - What work begins?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
RECOMMENDATION:
Option 2 - Dual-Segment Strategy is recommended.

RATIONALE:
Based on decision matrix, Option 2 scores 8.15/10 vs. 5.65 for Option 1
and 6.35 for Option 3. Primary reasons:
1. Captures substantial market opportunity (9/10 vs. 2/10 and 7/10)
2. Maintains financial attractiveness (8/10 across options)
3. Provides defensible competitive positioning (9/10)
4. While complex organizationally, risk is manageable with good governance

CONFIDENCE LEVEL: HIGH
- Market opportunity is well-researched and validated
- Financial case is solid across scenarios
- Organizational complexity is real but manageable
- Competitive timing is critical but window still open

WHAT WOULD CHANGE RECOMMENDATION:
- If we lose top SMB sales talent (makes Option 1 more attractive)
- If major enterprise customer signals concern about focus (Option 1)
- If we can't raise capital for investment (Option 1)
- If competitor moves extremely fast (might suggest Option 3 speed)

NEXT STEPS:
1. Executive decision on recommendation (this month)
2. If approved: Detailed execution planning for dual-segment strategy
3. Monthly governance review of progress
4. Gate review at 6 months to assess execution and adjust if needed
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfect certainty
- ‚ùå Unanimous stakeholder agreement
- ‚ùå Exhaustive contingency plans
- ‚ùå Complete implementation details

---

### 7. Documentation Quality & Clarity ‚úì

- [ ] **Matrix is visually clear**
  - Can someone understand matrix at a glance?
  - Rows and columns clear?
  - Scoring is easy to read?
  - No confusing formatting?

- [ ] **Supporting narrative is clear**
  - Can decision be understood without full context?
  - Scoring is explained?
  - Trade-offs are articulated?
  - Recommendation makes sense?

- [ ] **Document is well-organized**
  - Logical flow from criteria to scoring to recommendation
  - Key information is highlighted
  - Supporting detail is clear
  - Easy to find what you're looking for

**What This Looks Like:**
```
STRUCTURE:
1. Decision Question (¬Ω page)
2. Strategic Criteria & Weights (1 page)
3. Option Descriptions (1 page)
4. Decision Matrix with Scoring (1 page)
5. Trade-Offs Analysis (1 page)
6. Recommendation & Next Steps (¬Ω page)

CLARITY:
- Matrix uses consistent formatting
- Each score has supporting rationale
- Trade-offs are explicit
- Recommendation follows logically from matrix
- Easy for busy executives to understand

VISUAL:
- Table showing all options vs. criteria
- Visual representation of scores (1-10 scale)
- Highlights highest-scoring option
- Shows weighted scores clearly
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfect design
- ‚ùå Color-coded spreadsheets
- ‚ùå Complex visualizations
- ‚ùå Polished presentation formatting

---

## Enhancing Criteria (Improving Quality)

These items strengthen the matrix but aren't strictly blocking:

- [ ] **Stakeholder perspectives documented**
  - How would different executives weight criteria?
  - Are there legitimate disagreements?
  - How would stakeholders score options?
  - Are differences addressed in narrative?

- [ ] **Risk assessment integrated**
  - What are risks with each option?
  - How likely is risk to materialize?
  - What's the impact if risk happens?
  - How would we mitigate?

- [ ] **Implementation feasibility detailed**
  - For recommended option, what's required?
  - Do we have resources/capabilities?
  - What's the timeline?
  - What's the critical path?

- [ ] **Contingency planning included**
  - What if recommended option doesn't work?
  - What's plan B?
  - When would we pivot?
  - What triggers course correction?

- [ ] **Learning approach identified**
  - How will we test assumptions?
  - What would prove recommendation wrong?
  - How will we monitor and adjust?
  - What's decision point to reassess?

---

## Quality Checks (Before Approving)

Before confirming matrix is complete, ask yourself:

1. **Would decision-maker understand this?**
   - Can they see the options clearly?
   - Do they understand the criteria?
   - Can they see why recommendation emerges?
   - Would they have questions?

2. **Is recommendation defensible?**
   - Does it follow logically from matrix?
   - Would someone challenge the recommendation?
   - How would you respond to challenges?
   - Are trade-offs clear?

3. **Are all important factors considered?**
   - Are there criteria that should be included?
   - Are weighting realistic?
   - Would decision-makers agree with criteria?
   - Is anything missing?

4. **Could someone else score differently?**
   - Is scoring methodology clear?
   - Would scoring be similar?
   - Any subjective judgments?
   - How confident are we in scoring?

---

## Readiness Assessment

### ‚úÖ Matrix is Ready When

All blocking criteria are met:
- Decision question is clear
- Strategic options are distinct and complete
- Decision criteria are relevant and weighted
- Options are evaluated against criteria
- Trade-offs are explicitly addressed
- Recommendation is clear and justified
- Documentation quality is good

**Matrix can guide strategic decision-making.**

---

### üîÑ Matrix Needs More Work When

- **Decision unclear?** ‚Üí Clarify exactly what's being decided
- **Options not distinct?** ‚Üí Define truly different strategic paths
- **Criteria weak?** ‚Üí Ensure criteria align with what matters
- **Scoring inconsistent?** ‚Üí Clarify methodology; apply consistently
- **Trade-offs invisible?** ‚Üí Make explicit what we gain/lose
- **Recommendation unclear?** ‚Üí Show clearly which option wins and why

---

## Common Issues & Resolution

| Issue | Signal | Resolution |
|-------|--------|-----------|
| **Too many criteria** | "8 criteria to evaluate" | Consolidate to 4-6 key ones |
| **Criteria too vague** | "Can't score this" | Define specifically how to measure |
| **Scoring all similar** | "Everything scores 5-6" | Sharpen differentiation between options |
| **Matrix doesn't match recommendation** | "You said A but matrix shows B" | Fix scoring or rationale |
| **Weights seem arbitrary** | "Why is this 30%?" | Explain weighting rationale |
| **Missing obvious option** | "What about doing both?" | Add combination options |

---

## Post-Validation Actions

Once this checklist passes:

1. **Prepare for Decision**
   - Brief decision-maker on matrix
   - Prepare for likely questions
   - Confirm process and timeline
   - Identify any additional input needed

2. **Facilitate Decision**
   - Present matrix to executives
   - Walk through logic
   - Address questions and concerns
   - Get clear decision and approval

3. **Document Decision**
   - Record which option was chosen
   - Document rationale
   - Note any conditions or constraints
   - Plan for announcement to broader org

4. **Plan Implementation**
   - If Option 2: Begin execution planning
   - If Option 1: Continue current strategy
   - If Option 3: Begin acquisition/partnership process
   - Establish governance for execution

5. **Archive & Reference**
   - Save decision matrix
   - Document date of decision
   - Reference for future strategic decisions
   - Use as learning for future decisions

---

## Sign-Off

**Matrix Created By:** __________________ (Phase/Persona)

**Matrix Validated By:** __________________ (Reviewer)

==================== START: strategic-discovery-checklist ====================
# Strategic Discovery Readiness Checklist

## Purpose

Simple checklist for Strategic Orchestrator to assess readiness for strategic work handoff.

## Four Readiness Checks

### 1. Situation Clarity ‚úì

**Question:** "Do we understand the business context?"

- [ ] User can describe their business/organization
- [ ] User can explain their industry context  
- [ ] User can describe their current state/position
- [ ] Context is sufficient for persona selection

**NOT Required:**

- Complete business analysis
- Detailed competitive assessment
- Comprehensive organizational analysis

### 2. Challenge Definition ‚úì

**Question:** "Do we understand the problem or opportunity?"

- [ ] User can articulate what they're trying to address
- [ ] User can explain why this matters now
- [ ] User can describe the impact or importance
- [ ] Challenge is clear enough for strategic work to begin

**NOT Required:**

- Root cause analysis
- Quantified impact metrics
- Complete problem decomposition

### 3. Stakeholder Understanding ‚úì

**Question:** "Do we know who's involved and affected?"

- [ ] User knows who the key decision makers are
- [ ] User knows who would be involved in implementation
- [ ] User knows who would be affected by outcomes
- [ ] Stakeholder landscape is clear enough for strategic planning

**NOT Required:**

- Complete stakeholder mapping
- Detailed influence analysis
- Comprehensive change impact assessment

### 4. Outcome Expectations ‚úì

**Question:** "Do we understand what success looks like?"

- [ ] User has some vision of desired future state
- [ ] User can describe what they want to achieve
- [ ] User understands general success criteria
- [ ] Outcomes are clear enough to guide strategic work

**NOT Required:**

- Precise success metrics
- Detailed measurement frameworks
- Complete outcome specifications

## Overall Readiness Assessment

### ‚úÖ Ready to Proceed When

All four dimensions have basic clarity:

- User can describe their context (doesn't need to be complete)
- User can explain their challenge (doesn't need to be quantified)  
- User knows key stakeholders (doesn't need full mapping)
- User has outcome vision (doesn't need precise metrics)

### üîÑ Continue Discovery When

Major gaps exist in understanding:

- User unclear about basic business context
- User can't explain why challenge matters
- User doesn't know who's involved
- User has no sense of desired outcomes

### üë§ User Signals Override Assessment

Always respect user readiness signals:

- **"I think we have enough to get started"** ‚Üí Proceed
- **"Let me think about that more"** ‚Üí Give space
- **"I'm ready to dive in"** ‚Üí Proceed
- **"Can we explore this more?"** ‚Üí Continue discovery

## Mode-Specific Thresholds

### Express Mode

- Accept initial answers to four dimensions
- Light follow-up only if major gaps
- Proceed when basic understanding exists

### Deep Discovery Mode

- Thorough exploration of each dimension
- Multiple follow-ups as valuable
- User controls transition timing

## Quality Check Questions

Before handoff, ask yourself:

1. **Can I recommend the right strategic persona?** (based on context understanding)
2. **Can I create a useful Strategic Context Brief?** (enough substance to guide specialist)
3. **Will the specialist have enough context to start productively?** (sufficient but not complete)
4. **Does the user feel ready to proceed?** (respect their signals)

## What This Checklist Does NOT Require

‚ùå **Deep analysis** - That's for the specialist personas  
‚ùå **Problem solving** - That's for the strategic work phase  
‚ùå **Complete information** - Just enough for productive start  
‚ùå **Perfect clarity** - User may still have uncertainties  
‚ùå **Detailed planning** - That comes in strategic methodology  

## What This Checklist DOES Ensure

‚úÖ **Sufficient context** for smart persona selection  
‚úÖ **Productive handoff** to specialist personas  
‚úÖ **User readiness** for strategic work  
‚úÖ **Efficient use of time** - not over-discovering or under-preparing

==================== END: strategic-discovery-checklist ====================

==================== START: strategic-narrative-validation-checklist ====================
# Strategic Narrative Validation Checklist

## Purpose

Validate that a completed Strategic Narrative document presents a compelling, evidence-based story arc that engages stakeholders and drives decision-making through narrative structure and persuasive communication.

## What Gets Validated

**Template Reference:** `templates/strategic-narrative.md`

**Used In:** STORY phase for narrative development

**Audience:** Executives, board, stakeholders reviewing strategic narrative

## Blocking Criteria (All Required)

### 1. Clear Story Arc ‚úì

- [ ] **Opening sets context and hooks attention**
  - Problem/opportunity presented compellingly
  - Why audience should care
  - Stakes are clear
  - Invitation to journey

- [ ] **Middle builds evidence and logic**
  - Analysis presented in digestible form
  - Findings build toward conclusion
  - Evidence woven throughout (not separated)
  - Logical progression from facts to implications

- [ ] **Closing shows implications and calls to action**
  - What findings mean for future
  - Decision or action required
  - Clear recommendation or options
  - Path forward articulated

**What This Looks Like:**
```
OPENING:
"Our enterprise business is thriving. We've built a category-leading
platform trusted by Fortune 500 companies. $15M ARR. 95% retention.
We're winning in the market we've chosen. But here's the problem:
in winning the enterprise market, we've missed a much larger opportunity
in the SMB market growing next door. A $2B market is growing at 25%
annually. Competitors are entering. Our window to claim significant
share is closing. This isn't a problem to fix. It's a strategic choice."

MIDDLE:
"Let me show you what's happening. Our SMB customers are churning at
15% annually‚Äîdouble our enterprise rate. Why? Primarily price.
We're a $10K-50K solution; SMB wants something at $1K-5K. It's not
that our product isn't good; it's that it's over-engineered for their
needs. Meanwhile, competitors are doing exactly what we're avoiding:
serving the SMB market with simplified, affordable solutions.

But here's where this gets interesting. While we were perfecting
enterprise, something changed in the market. SMB moved from 'me too'
market followers to primary market. And SMB customers increasingly
want what enterprise customers have: integrations, reliability,
predictability. But at SMB prices and simplicity.

We have something competitors don't: proven enterprise success.
Reference customers. Product quality. Retention rates that prove
value. If we could bring enterprise-grade reliability to SMB-friendly
simplicity and pricing, we'd have something unique."

CLOSING:
"So the choice is clear. We can continue our enterprise-only strategy.
It's proven. It's profitable. But it leaves $2B on the table and makes
us vulnerable if competitors move upmarket and drive enterprise pricing
pressure.

Or we can pursue a dual-segment strategy. Separate products. Separate
go-to-market. Separate teams. One focused on enterprise excellence.
One focused on SMB accessibility. It's more complex. It requires
discipline. But it doubles our addressable market and positions us
for market leadership.

The data supports the second path. The market supports it. And the
opportunity window says we need to decide‚Äîand begin executing‚Äîsoon.

What I need from you: approval to pursue dual-segment strategy and
$5M investment to execute over 18 months. What you'll get: path to
$40M+ revenue by Year 3 and defensible market leadership position."
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfect storytelling
- ‚ùå Elaborate narrative devices
- ‚ùå Emotional manipulation
- ‚ùå Complete elimination of jargon

---

### 2. Evidence Woven into Narrative ‚úì

- [ ] **Findings are presented, not appended**
  - Data is integrated into story
  - Evidence supports claims
  - Numbers used when they matter
  - Sources transparent

- [ ] **Claims are supported by evidence**
  - "SMB customers churn at 15%" supported by data
  - "Competitors moving downmarket" with examples
  - "Market growing 25%" with source
  - No unsupported assertions

- [ ] **Confidence levels are clear**
  - What we're certain about vs. indicative
  - Where data is strong vs. exploratory
  - Caveats and limitations noted
  - Audience can assess credibility

**What This Looks Like:**
```
CLAIM: "SMB customers are churning at 15% annually"
EVIDENCE: "Our CRM data shows 40 of 250 SMB customers churned over
past 12 months. That's 16% actual rate. For comparison, enterprise
churn is 5%."
CONFIDENCE: "HIGH - this is our actual data"

CLAIM: "Price is the primary driver of SMB churn"
EVIDENCE: "We conducted exit interviews with 15 churned SMB customers.
Twelve cited price as primary reason. We also analyzed customer
success notes showing price concerns appear in 70% of at-risk accounts."
CONFIDENCE: "MODERATE - based on interviews and notes. Actual impact
could vary, but trend is clear."

CLAIM: "SMB market is $2B TAM growing at 25%"
EVIDENCE: "Third-party market research firm (Gartner, August 2024)
estimates $2B TAM. Multiple sources show 20-30% annual growth rates."
CONFIDENCE: "MODERATE - secondary research. Market moving fast.
Estimates vary $1.5B-3B depending on definition."
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Every claim cited
- ‚ùå Appendix of sources
- ‚ùå Academic-level documentation
- ‚ùå Perfect confidence assessment

---

### 3. Emotional Resonance Alongside Logic ‚úì

- [ ] **Story connects intellectually and emotionally**
  - Logic appeals to head
  - Stakes/implications appeal to heart
  - Audience cares about decision
  - Personal connection to outcome visible

- [ ] **Language is engaging and clear**
  - Not overly technical or academic
  - Active voice where possible
  - Concrete examples and specifics
  - Accessible to audience

- [ ] **Narrative momentum builds**
  - Opening creates curiosity
  - Middle sustains interest
  - Closing creates sense of urgency/opportunity
  - Audience wants to hear more

**What This Looks Like:**
```
EMOTIONAL APPEAL (not manipulation):

"Imagine you're a growing tech company with $2M in funding. You need
software to manage customer data and integrations. You find two options:

Option A: The enterprise solution. Feature-rich. Powerful. Reliable.
$50K annual contract. Requires implementation team. Learning curve.

Option B: The SMB solution. Simpler. Easier to use. $2K annual contract.
You're up and running in days.

You choose Option B. Not because it's inferior. But because it's
'good enough' for what you need. And the price and simplicity matter.

This is happening thousands of times in the SMB market right now.
And we're not winning those battles because we're Option A. We've
optimized for Option A. And while Option A is profitable, Option B
is where the market is moving."

LOGIC + EMOTION:
Intellectual: Market dynamics and opportunity size
Emotional: Recognition of missed customers, competitive threat, opportunity
Together: Sense that this is both right AND urgent
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Personal stories or anecdotes (not required)
- ‚ùå Dramatic language or hyperbole
- ‚ùå Theatrical presentation
- ‚ùå Perfect balance of head/heart

---

### 4. Clear Decision or Action Called For ‚úì

- [ ] **What's being asked is explicit**
  - What decision is needed?
  - What's the specific ask?
  - Timeline for decision
  - What happens next

- [ ] **Options are presented (if decision)**
  - What choices exist?
  - What would each mean?
  - What's recommended?
  - Why that recommendation?

- [ ] **Path forward is clear**
  - What happens if we decide yes?
  - What resources needed?
  - What's timeline?
  - What's success look like?

**What This Looks Like:**
```
THE ASK:
"I'm asking for two things:

1. Strategic approval to pursue a dual-segment strategy (enterprise +
SMB). This means separate products, separate teams, separate go-to-market.
Enterprise remains core focus and investment. SMB is additive opportunity.

2. Budget approval of $5M over 18 months to execute this strategy:
- $3M for product development (SMB product, simplified architecture)
- $1M for go-to-market (sales team, partnerships, marketing)
- $1M for operations (customer success, infrastructure)

WHAT YOU'RE SAYING YES TO:
- Company becomes dual-segment operator (not venture scale-at-all-costs
  thinking, but profitable-growth mindset)
- R&D splits focus between enterprise evolution and SMB new product
- 18-month execution timeline to validate SMB viability
- Monthly reviews and milestone gates to ensure execution quality

WHAT YOU GET:
- Path to $40M+ revenue by Year 3 (vs. $25M enterprise-only)
- Market leadership position in both segments
- Defensible competitive moat (can't be beaten by pure price or pure
  feature plays)
- Optionality: if SMB works, we scale. If it struggles, we have proven
  enterprise business that remains strong.

TIMELINE:
- Decision: This month
- Execution start: Next month
- Phase 1 (Foundation): Months 1-6
- Phase 2 (Launch): Months 6-12
- Phase 3 (Scale): Months 12-18
- Review point: Month 18 (full assessment of viability)
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Detailed implementation plan
- ‚ùå Complete business case
- ‚ùå Every possible scenario
- ‚ùå Risk mitigation for all risks

---

### 5. Audience Engagement & Accessibility ‚úì

- [ ] **Narrative is appropriate for audience**
  - Executive audience: Focus on strategy and financials
  - Board audience: Focus on opportunity and risk
  - Operational audience: Focus on implications
  - Right level of detail

- [ ] **Jargon is minimized or explained**
  - Technical terms explained
  - Acronyms defined
  - Industry terms contextualized
  - Accessible to non-experts

- [ ] **Narrative invites dialogue**
  - Audience feels heard
  - Concerns are acknowledged
  - Alternative views are respected
  - Invitation to challenge/question

**What This Looks Like:**
```
FOR EXECUTIVE TEAM:
Focus on: Strategy, market opportunity, competitive positioning,
financial return, execution risk
Tone: Data-driven, business-focused, action-oriented
Key questions addressed: "Why now?" "Why this approach?" "What could
go wrong?" "What do we get?"

FOR BOARD OF DIRECTORS:
Focus on: Market opportunity, competitive positioning, financial
returns, risk management, governance
Tone: Rigorous, balanced, professional
Key questions addressed: "Is this a good market?" "Can we win?"
"What's the return?" "What are the risks?"

FOR OPERATIONS TEAM:
Focus on: What this means for our team, what will change, what
resources needed, how we'll be involved
Tone: Collaborative, realistic about challenges, supportive
Key questions addressed: "How does this affect my area?" "What am I
being asked to do?" "Will we have resources?" "How will success be
measured?"

ACCESSIBILITY:
Minimize: TAM/SAM/SOM jargon (define as market opportunity)
Minimize: Technical product terms (explain in business terms)
Explain: Market segments (enterprise vs. SMB, what's different)
Explain: Business model implications (how profitability will work)
Invite: Questions and challenges ("What would change your mind?")
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Separate narratives for each group (one good narrative works)
- ‚ùå Oversimplification
- ‚ùå Elimination of complexity
- ‚ùå Complete dumbing down

---

### 6. Quality of Expression ‚úì

- [ ] **Writing is clear and professional**
  - Grammar and spelling correct
  - Sentences are clear (not convoluted)
  - Paragraphs are well-structured
  - Ideas flow logically

- [ ] **Narrative is appropriately formatted**
  - Easy to follow structure
  - Sections are clear
  - Key points highlighted
  - Not too dense (good white space)

- [ ] **Tone is appropriate**
  - Professional but not stuffy
  - Confident but not arrogant
  - Clear but not simplistic
  - Engaging but not dramatic

**What This Looks Like:**
```
STRUCTURE:
1. Opening (Hook & Context) - ¬Ω page
2. The Situation (What's happening?) - 1-2 pages
3. The Analysis (What did we learn?) - 2-3 pages
4. The Implications (What does it mean?) - 1 page
5. The Recommendation (What should we do?) - 1 page
6. The Path Forward (What happens next?) - ¬Ω page

TOTAL: 6-8 pages (executive readable in 20-30 minutes)

TONE EXAMPLES:

Good: "SMB customers are churning at twice the rate of enterprise
customers, primarily because our pricing is misaligned with SMB budgets."
(Professional, clear, data-driven)

Not great: "Our SMB business is like a leaky bucket. Customers pour in,
but they're dripping out constantly." (Too metaphorical for executive
briefing)

Good: "We have an opportunity to capture $2B market while defending our
profitable enterprise position. The market window is likely 12-18 months
before competitive consolidation. Decision is strategic, not tactical."
(Clear stakes, urgency, significance)

Not great: "This is HUGE. We need to move NOW or we'll regret it forever!"
(Hyperbolic, less credible)
```

**What This Does NOT Require:**
- ‚ùå Perfectly polished prose
- ‚ùå Elaborate sentence structure
- ‚ùå Perfect word choice
- ‚ùå Poetic language

---

## Enhancing Criteria (Improving Quality)

These items strengthen the narrative but aren't strictly blocking:

- [ ] **Customer voice is prominent**
  - Customer quotes included
  - Customer pain points visible
  - Customer perspective on solution
  - Makes narrative personal

- [ ] **Scenario planning included**
  - What if we do this? What happens?
  - What if we don't? What happens?
  - What if competitors respond? How?
  - Helps audience imagine future

- [ ] **Visual elements support narrative**
  - Key charts/graphics included
  - Visuals tell story (not just pretty)
  - Flow from one to next
  - Professional quality

- [ ] **Objections/concerns proactively addressed**
  - What might people worry about?
  - How does narrative address concerns?
  - Where are vulnerabilities?
  - How do we mitigate?

---

## Quality Checks

Before confirming narrative is complete, ask yourself:

1. **Would this persuade the audience?**
   - Would they understand the opportunity?
   - Would they see why action is needed?
   - Would they believe the recommendation?
   - Would they want to support it?

2. **Is narrative credible?**
   - Based on solid evidence?
   - Free from exaggeration?
   - Acknowledges uncertainty?
   - Would skeptics find it credible?

3. **Is narrative memorable?**
   - Key message would stick?
   - Could someone tell it to someone else?
   - Evidence is memorable?
   - Call-to-action is clear?

4. **Is narrative appropriate?**
   - Right for audience?
   - Right level of detail?
   - Right tone?
   - Right for context?

---

## Readiness Assessment

### ‚úÖ Narrative is Ready When

All blocking criteria are met:
- Clear story arc from opening to closing
- Evidence woven into narrative
- Emotional resonance alongside logic
- Decision or action called for
- Audience engagement and accessibility
- Quality of expression is professional

**Narrative can persuade and drive decision-making.**

---

## Common Issues & Resolution

| Issue | Signal | Resolution |
|-------|--------|-----------|
| **Too long** | "10+ pages" | Cut to 6-8 pages; move detail to appendix |
| **Too abstract** | "Can't picture what you mean" | Add concrete examples and specifics |
| **No clear ask** | "What are we deciding?" | Explicitly state what decision/action needed |
| **Evidence buried** | "Where's the proof?" | Weave evidence throughout; don't append |
| **Too emotional** | "Feels like sales pitch" | Tone down hyperbole; balance with logic |
| **Not persuasive** | "Still not convinced" | Strengthen evidence; address objections |

---

## Post-Validation Actions

Once this checklist passes:

1. **Prepare for Presentation**
   - Practice delivering narrative
   - Prepare for likely questions
   - Create presentation slides if needed
   - Brief decision-maker on approach

2. **Refine Based on Feedback**
   - Share with trusted reviewers
   - Incorporate feedback
   - Adjust tone if needed
   - Finalize narrative

3. **Use for Communication**
   - Present narrative to decision-maker(s)
   - Share with broader stakeholders
   - Adapt for different audiences
   - Use for ongoing communication

4. **Archive & Reference**
   - Save final narrative
   - Document decision date
   - Use for learning (what worked?)
   - Reference for future narratives

---

## Sign-Off

**Narrative Created By:** __________________ (Phase/Persona)

**Narrative Validated By:** __________________ (Reviewer)

**Audience Confirmed:** __________________ (Decision-maker)

**Date Validated:** __________________

**Confirmed Ready for Presentation:** ‚òê Yes ‚òê No
